79𓀴
80𓀴Chapter 1.
81𓀴Mr. Sherlock Holmes
82𓀴
83𓀴
84𓀴      Mr. Sherlock Holmes, who was
 usually very late in the mornings,
85𓀴      save upon those
 not infrequent
 occasions when he was up all
86𓀴      night, was seated
 at the breakfast
 table. I stood upon the
87𓀴      hearth-rug and picked up the
 stick which our visitor had left
88𓀴      behind him the night before.
 It was a fine, thick piece of wood,
89𓀴      bulbous-headed,
 of the sort which
 is known as a “Penang lawyer.”
90𓀴      Just under the head was a
 broad silver band nearly an inch
91𓀴      across. “To James Mortimer,
 M.R.C.S., from his friends of the
92𓀴      C.C.H.,” was
 engraved upon it,
 with the date “1884.” It was just
93𓀴      such a stick
 as the old-fashioned
 family practitioner used to
94𓀴      carry—dignified,
 solid, and reassuring.
95𓀴
96𓀴      “Well, Watson, what
 do you make of it?”
97𓀴
98𓀴      Holmes was sitting with his
 back to me, and I had given him no
99𓀴      sign of my occupation.
100𓀴
101𓀴      “How did you know what I was
 doing? I believe you have eyes in
102𓀴      the back of your head.”
103𓀴
104𓀴      “I have, at least,
 a well-polished,
 silver-plated coffee-pot in
105𓀴      front of me,” said he. “But,
 tell me, Watson, what do you make of
106𓀴      our visitor’s
 stick? Since we
 have been so unfortunate as to miss
107𓀴      him and have
 no notion of his
 errand, this accidental souvenir
108𓀴      becomes of
 importance. Let me
 hear you reconstruct the man by an
109𓀴      examination of it.”
110𓀴
111𓀴      “I think,” said I, following
 as far as I could the methods of my
112𓀴      companion,
 “that Dr. Mortimer
 is a successful, elderly medical
113𓀴      man, well-esteemed
 since those
 who know him give him this mark of
114𓀴      their appreciation.”
115𓀴
116𓀴      “Good!” said
 Holmes. “Excellent!”
117𓀴
118𓀴      “I think also
 that the probability
 is in favour of his being a
119𓀴      country practitioner
 who does
 a great deal of his visiting on
120𓀴      foot.”
121𓀴
122𓀴      “Why so?”
123𓀴
124𓀴      “Because this stick, though
 originally a very handsome one has
125𓀴      been so knocked about that
 I can hardly imagine a town
126𓀴      practitioner
 carrying it. The
 thick-iron ferrule is worn down, so
127𓀴      it is evident
 that he has done
 a great amount of walking with
128𓀴      it.”
129𓀴
130𓀴      “Perfectly sound!”
 said Holmes.
131𓀴
132𓀴      “And then again,
 there is the
 ‘friends of the C.C.H.’ I should
133𓀴      guess that
 to be the Something
 Hunt, the local hunt to whose
134𓀴      members he
 has possibly given
 some surgical assistance, and which
135𓀴      has made him a small
 presentation in return.”
136𓀴
137𓀴      “Really, Watson,
 you excel yourself,”
 said Holmes, pushing back
138𓀴      his chair and
 lighting a cigarette.
 “I am bound to say that in
139𓀴      all the accounts
 which you have
 been so good as to give of my own
140𓀴      small achievements you have
 habitually underrated your own
141𓀴      abilities.
 It may be that you
 are not yourself luminous, but you
142𓀴      are a conductor
 of light. Some
 people without possessing genius
143𓀴      have a remarkable power of
 stimulating it. I confess, my dear
144𓀴      fellow, that I am
 very much in your debt.”
145𓀴
146𓀴      He had never
 said as much before,
 and I must admit that his words
147𓀴      gave me keen pleasure, for
 I had often been piqued by his
148𓀴      indifference
 to my admiration
 and to the attempts which I had
149𓀴      made to give
 publicity to his
 methods. I was proud, too, to think
150𓀴      that I had so far mastered
 his system as to apply it in a way
151𓀴      which earned his approval.
 He now took the stick from my hands
152𓀴      and examined
 it for a few minutes
 with his naked eyes. Then with
153𓀴      an expression of interest
 he laid down his cigarette, and
154𓀴      carrying the
 cane to the window,
 he looked over it again with a
155𓀴      convex lens.
156𓀴
157𓀴      “Interesting,
 though elementary,”
 said he as he returned to his
158𓀴      favourite corner
 of the settee.
 “There are certainly one or two
159𓀴      indications upon the stick.
 It gives us the basis for several
160𓀴      deductions.”
161𓀴
162𓀴      “Has anything escaped me?” I
 asked with some self-importance. “I
163𓀴      trust that there is nothing
 of consequence which I have
164𓀴      overlooked?”
165𓀴
166𓀴      “I am afraid,
 my dear Watson,
 that most of your conclusions were
167𓀴      erroneous. When I said that
 you stimulated me I meant, to be
168𓀴      frank, that in noting your
 fallacies I was occasionally guided
169𓀴      towards the truth. Not that
 you are entirely wrong in this
170𓀴      instance. The
 man is certainly
 a country practitioner. And he
171𓀴      walks a good deal.”
172𓀴
173𓀴      “Then I was right.”
174𓀴
175𓀴      “To that extent.”
176𓀴
177𓀴      “But that was all.”
178𓀴
179𓀴      “No, no, my dear Watson,
 not all—by no means all. I would
180𓀴      suggest, for example, that
 a presentation to a doctor is more
181𓀴      likely to come
 from a hospital
 than from a hunt, and that when
182𓀴      the initials
 ‘C.C.’ are placed
 before that hospital the words
183𓀴      ‘Charing Cross’
 very naturally
 suggest themselves.”
184𓀴
185𓀴      “You may be right.”
186𓀴
187𓀴      “The probability
 lies in that
 direction. And if we take this as a
188𓀴      working hypothesis we have a
 fresh basis from which to start our
189𓀴      construction of
 this unknown visitor.”
190𓀴
191𓀴      “Well, then, supposing that
 ‘C.C.H.’ does stand for ‘Charing
192𓀴      Cross Hospital,’
 what further
 inferences may we draw?”
193𓀴
194𓀴      “Do none suggest themselves?
 You know my methods. Apply them!”
195𓀴
196𓀴      “I can only
 think of the obvious
 conclusion that the man has
197𓀴      practised in town before
 going to the country.”
198𓀴
199𓀴      “I think that
 we might venture
 a little farther than this. Look
200𓀴      at it in this light. On what
 occasion would it be most probable
201𓀴      that such a
 presentation would
 be made? When would his friends
202𓀴      unite to give
 him a pledge of
 their good will? Obviously at the
203𓀴      moment when Dr. Mortimer
 withdrew from the service of the
204𓀴      hospital in order to start a
 practice for himself. We know there
205𓀴      has been a presentation. We
 believe there has been a change from
206𓀴      a town hospital to a country
 practice. Is it, then, stretching
207𓀴      our inference too far to say
 that the presentation was on the
208𓀴      occasion of the change?”
209𓀴
210𓀴      “It certainly
 seems probable.”
211𓀴
212𓀴      “Now, you will
 observe that he
 could not have been on the _staff_
213𓀴      of the hospital, since only
 a man well-established in a London
214𓀴      practice could hold such a
 position, and such a one would not
215𓀴      drift into the country. What
 was he, then? If he was in the
216𓀴      hospital and yet not on the
 staff he could only have been a
217𓀴      house-surgeon or
 a house-physician—little
 more than a senior
218𓀴      student. And
 he left five years
 ago—the date is on the stick. So
219𓀴      your grave,
 middle-aged family
 practitioner vanishes into thin
220𓀴      air, my dear
 Watson, and there
 emerges a young fellow under
221𓀴      thirty, amiable,
 unambitious,
 absent-minded, and the possessor of
222𓀴      a favourite
 dog, which I should
 describe roughly as being larger
223𓀴      than a terrier and
 smaller than a mastiff.”
224𓀴
225𓀴      I laughed incredulously as
 Sherlock Holmes leaned back in his
226𓀴      settee and blew
 little wavering
 rings of smoke up to the ceiling.
227𓀴
228𓀴      “As to the
 latter part, I have
 no means of checking you,” said I,
229𓀴      “but at least
 it is not difficult
 to find out a few particulars
230𓀴      about the man’s
 age and professional
 career.” From my small
231𓀴      medical shelf
 I took down the
 Medical Directory and turned up the
232𓀴      name. There were
 several Mortimers,
 but only one who could be our
233𓀴      visitor. I read
 his record aloud.
234𓀴
235𓀴        “Mortimer,
 James, M.R.C.S.,
 1882, Grimpen, Dartmoor, Devon.
236𓀴        House-surgeon, from 1882
 to 1884, at Charing Cross Hospital.
237𓀴        Winner of
 the Jackson prize
 for Comparative Pathology, with
238𓀴        essay entitled ‘Is Disease
 a Reversion?’  Corresponding member
239𓀴        of the Swedish
 Pathological
 Society.  Author of ‘Some Freaks of
240𓀴        Atavism’ (_Lancet_ 1882).
  ‘Do We Progress?’ (_Journal of
241𓀴        Psychology_, March, 1883).
 Medical Officer for the parishes of
242𓀴        Grimpen, Thorsley,
 and High Barrow.”
243𓀴
244𓀴      “No mention of that local
 hunt, Watson,” said Holmes with a
245𓀴      mischievous smile,
 “but a country
 doctor, as you very astutely
246𓀴      observed. I think
 that I am fairly
 justified in my inferences. As
247𓀴      to the adjectives, I said,
 if I remember right, amiable,
248𓀴      unambitious,
 and absent-minded.
 It is my experience that it is
249𓀴      only an amiable
 man in this world
 who receives testimonials, only
250𓀴      an unambitious
 one who abandons
 a London career for the country,
251𓀴      and only an
 absent-minded one
 who leaves his stick and not his
252𓀴      visiting-card after waiting
 an hour in your room.”
253𓀴
254𓀴      “And the dog?”
255𓀴
256𓀴      “Has been in
 the habit of carrying
 this stick behind his master.
257𓀴      Being a heavy stick the dog
 has held it tightly by the middle,
258𓀴      and the marks of his teeth
 are very plainly visible. The dog’s
259𓀴      jaw, as shown
 in the space between
 these marks, is too broad in
260𓀴      my opinion for a terrier and
 not broad enough for a mastiff. It
261𓀴      may have been—yes, by Jove,
 it _is_ a curly-haired spaniel.”
262𓀴
263𓀴      He had risen
 and paced the room
 as he spoke. Now he halted in the
264𓀴      recess of the window. There
 was such a ring of conviction in his
265𓀴      voice that I glanced
 up in surprise.
266𓀴
267𓀴      “My dear fellow, how can
 you possibly be so sure of that?”
268𓀴
269𓀴      “For the very simple reason
 that I see the dog himself on our
270𓀴      very door-step, and there is
 the ring of its owner. Don’t move, I
271𓀴      beg you, Watson.
 He is a professional
 brother of yours, and your
272𓀴      presence may
 be of assistance
 to me. Now is the dramatic moment
273𓀴      of fate, Watson,
 when you hear
 a step upon the stair which is
274𓀴      walking into
 your life, and you
 know not whether for good or ill.
275𓀴      What does Dr.
 James Mortimer,
 the man of science, ask of Sherlock
276𓀴      Holmes, the specialist
 in crime? Come in!”
277𓀴
278𓀴      The appearance
 of our visitor
 was a surprise to me, since I had
279𓀴      expected a typical
 country practitioner.
 He was a very tall, thin
280𓀴      man, with a long nose like a
 beak, which jutted out between two
281𓀴      keen, grey eyes, set closely
 together and sparkling brightly from
282𓀴      behind a pair of gold-rimmed
 glasses. He was clad in a
283𓀴      professional
 but rather slovenly
 fashion, for his frock-coat was
284𓀴      dingy and his
 trousers frayed.
 Though young, his long back was
285𓀴      already bowed, and he walked
 with a forward thrust of his head
286𓀴      and a general air of peering
 benevolence. As he entered his eyes
287𓀴      fell upon the
 stick in Holmes’s
 hand, and he ran towards it with
288𓀴      an exclamation of joy. “I am
 so very glad,” said he. “I was not
289𓀴      sure whether I had left it
 here or in the Shipping Office. I
290𓀴      would not lose that
 stick for the world.”
291𓀴
292𓀴      “A presentation,
 I see,” said Holmes.
293𓀴
294𓀴      “Yes, sir.”
295𓀴
296𓀴      “From Charing
 Cross Hospital?”
297𓀴
298𓀴      “From one or
 two friends there
 on the occasion of my marriage.”
299𓀴
300𓀴      “Dear, dear, that’s bad!”
 said Holmes, shaking his head.
301𓀴
302𓀴      Dr. Mortimer blinked through
 his glasses in mild astonishment.
303𓀴      “Why was it bad?”
304𓀴
305𓀴      “Only that you
 have disarranged
 our little deductions. Your
306𓀴      marriage, you say?”
307𓀴
308𓀴      “Yes, sir. I married, and so
 left the hospital, and with it all
309𓀴      hopes of a consulting
 practice.
 It was necessary to make a home
310𓀴      of my own.”
311𓀴
312𓀴      “Come, come, we are not so
 far wrong, after all,” said Holmes.
313𓀴      “And now, Dr.
 James Mortimer—”
314𓀴
315𓀴      “Mister, sir, Mister—a
 humble M.R.C.S.”
316𓀴
317𓀴      “And a man of precise
 mind, evidently.”
318𓀴
319𓀴      “A dabbler in
 science, Mr. Holmes,
 a picker up of shells on the
320𓀴      shores of the great unknown
 ocean. I presume that it is Mr.
321𓀴      Sherlock Holmes whom
 I am addressing and not—”
322𓀴
323𓀴      “No, this is my
 friend Dr. Watson.”
324𓀴
325𓀴      “Glad to meet you, sir. I
 have heard your name mentioned in
326𓀴      connection with that of your
 friend. You interest me very much,
327𓀴      Mr. Holmes. I
 had hardly expected
 so dolichocephalic a skull or
328𓀴      such well-marked
 supra-orbital
 development. Would you have any
329𓀴      objection to
 my running my finger
 along your parietal fissure? A
330𓀴      cast of your
 skull, sir, until
 the original is available, would
331𓀴      be an ornament
 to any anthropological
 museum. It is not my
332𓀴      intention to be fulsome, but
 I confess that I covet your skull.”
333𓀴
334𓀴      Sherlock Holmes
 waved our strange
 visitor into a chair. “You are
335𓀴      an enthusiast
 in your line of
 thought, I perceive, sir, as I am
336𓀴      in mine,” said
 he. “I observe
 from your forefinger that you make
337𓀴      your own cigarettes. Have
 no hesitation in lighting one.”
338𓀴
339𓀴      The man drew
 out paper and tobacco
 and twirled the one up in the
340𓀴      other with surprising
 dexterity.
 He had long, quivering fingers
341𓀴      as agile and restless
 as the antennæ of an insect.
342𓀴
343𓀴      Holmes was silent,
 but his little
 darting glances showed me the
344𓀴      interest which he took in
 our curious companion. “I presume,
345𓀴      sir,” said he at last, “that
 it was not merely for the purpose of
346𓀴      examining my skull that you
 have done me the honour to call here
347𓀴      last night and again today?”
348𓀴
349𓀴      “No, sir, no;
 though I am happy
 to have had the opportunity of
350𓀴      doing that as well. I came
 to you, Mr. Holmes, because I
351𓀴      recognized that I am myself
 an unpractical man and because I am
352𓀴      suddenly confronted with
 a most serious and extraordinary
353𓀴      problem. Recognizing, as I
 do, that you are the second highest
354𓀴      expert in Europe—”
355𓀴
356𓀴      “Indeed, sir! May I inquire
 who has the honour to be the first?”
357𓀴      asked Holmes
 with some asperity.
358𓀴
359𓀴      “To the man of
 precisely scientific
 mind the work of Monsieur
360𓀴      Bertillon must always
 appeal strongly.”
361𓀴
362𓀴      “Then had you not
 better consult him?”
363𓀴
364𓀴      “I said, sir,
 to the precisely
 scientific mind. But as a
365𓀴      practical man
 of affairs it is
 acknowledged that you stand alone.
366𓀴      I trust, sir, that
 I have not inadvertently—”
367𓀴
368𓀴      “Just a little,”
 said Holmes.
 “I think, Dr. Mortimer, you would
369𓀴      do wisely if
 without more ado
 you would kindly tell me plainly
370𓀴      what the exact nature of the
 problem is in which you demand my
371𓀴      assistance.”
372𓀴
373𓀴
374𓀴
375𓀴
376𓀴Chapter 2.
377𓀴The Curse of the Baskervilles
378𓀴
379𓀴
380𓀴      “I have in my
 pocket a manuscript,”
 said Dr. James Mortimer.
381𓀴
382𓀴      “I observed
 it as you entered
 the room,” said Holmes.
383𓀴
384𓀴      “It is an old manuscript.”
385𓀴
386𓀴      “Early eighteenth century,
 unless it is a forgery.”
387𓀴
388𓀴      “How can you say that, sir?”
389𓀴
390𓀴      “You have presented an inch
 or two of it to my examination all
391𓀴      the time that you have been
 talking. It would be a poor expert
392𓀴      who could not give the date
 of a document within a decade or so.
393𓀴      You may possibly
 have read my
 little monograph upon the subject.
394𓀴      I put that at 1730.”
395𓀴
396𓀴      “The exact date is 1742.”
 Dr. Mortimer drew it from his
397𓀴      breast-pocket.
 “This family paper
 was committed to my care by Sir
398𓀴      Charles Baskerville, whose
 sudden and tragic death some three
399𓀴      months ago created so much
 excitement in Devonshire. I may say
400𓀴      that I was
 his personal friend
 as well as his medical attendant.
401𓀴      He was a strong-minded man,
 sir, shrewd, practical, and as
402𓀴      unimaginative
 as I am myself.
 Yet he took this document very
403𓀴      seriously, and his mind was
 prepared for just such an end as did
404𓀴      eventually overtake him.”
405𓀴
406𓀴      Holmes stretched
 out his hand
 for the manuscript and flattened it
407𓀴      upon his knee.
 “You will observe,
 Watson, the alternative use of
408𓀴      the long _s_ and the short.
 It is one of several indications
409𓀴      which enabled
 me to fix the date.”
410𓀴
411𓀴      I looked over his shoulder
 at the yellow paper and the faded
412𓀴      script. At the
 head was written:
 “Baskerville Hall,” and below in
413𓀴      large, scrawling
 figures: “1742.”
414𓀴
415𓀴      “It appears to be
 a statement of some sort.”
416𓀴
417𓀴      “Yes, it is a statement of
 a certain legend which runs in the
418𓀴      Baskerville family.”
419𓀴
420𓀴      “But I understand that it is
 something more modern and practical
421𓀴      upon which you
 wish to consult me?”
422𓀴
423𓀴      “Most modern.
 A most practical,
 pressing matter, which must be
424𓀴      decided within
 twenty-four hours.
 But the manuscript is short and
425𓀴      is intimately connected with
 the affair. With your permission I
426𓀴      will read it to you.”
427𓀴
428𓀴      Holmes leaned
 back in his chair,
 placed his finger-tips together,
429𓀴      and closed his eyes, with an
 air of resignation. Dr. Mortimer
430𓀴      turned the manuscript to the
 light and read in a high, cracking
431𓀴      voice the following curious,
 old-world narrative:
432𓀴
433𓀴        “Of the origin
 of the Hound
 of the Baskervilles there have been
434𓀴        many statements, yet as
 I come in a direct line from Hugo
435𓀴        Baskerville, and as I had
 the story from my father, who also
436𓀴        had it from
 his, I have set
 it down with all belief that it
437𓀴        occurred even as is here
 set forth. And I would have you
438𓀴        believe, my sons, that the
 same Justice which punishes sin may
439𓀴        also most
 graciously forgive
 it, and that no ban is so heavy
440𓀴        but that by
 prayer and repentance
 it may be removed.  Learn
441𓀴        then from this story not
 to fear the fruits of the past, but
442𓀴        rather to be circumspect
 in the future, that those foul
443𓀴        passions
 whereby our family
 has suffered so grievously may not
444𓀴        again be loosed
 to our undoing.
445𓀴
446𓀴            “Know then that in the
 time of the Great Rebellion (the
447𓀴            history of which by
 the learned Lord Clarendon I most
448𓀴            earnestly commend
 to your attention) this Manor of
449𓀴            Baskerville was held
 by Hugo of that name, nor can it be
450𓀴            gainsaid that he was a
 most wild, profane, and godless man.
451𓀴             This, in
 truth, his neighbours
 might have pardoned, seeing
452𓀴            that saints have never
 flourished in those parts, but there
453𓀴            was in
 him a certain wanton
 and cruel humour which made his
454𓀴            name a by-word through
 the West.  It chanced that this Hugo
455𓀴            came to
 love (if, indeed,
 so dark a passion may be known
456𓀴            under
 so bright a name)
 the daughter of a yeoman who held
457𓀴            lands
 near the Baskerville
 estate. But the young maiden,
458𓀴            being discreet and of
 good repute, would ever avoid him,
459𓀴            for she
 feared his evil
 name.  So it came to pass that one
460𓀴            Michaelmas this Hugo,
 with five or six of his idle and
461𓀴            wicked
 companions, stole
 down upon the farm and carried off
462𓀴            the maiden, her father
 and brothers being from home, as he
463𓀴            well knew.  When they
 had brought her to the Hall the
464𓀴            maiden
 was placed in an
 upper chamber, while Hugo and his
465𓀴            friends sat down to a
 long carouse, as was their nightly
466𓀴            custom.  Now, the poor
 lass upstairs was like to have her
467𓀴            wits turned
 at the singing
 and shouting and terrible oaths
468𓀴            which came up to her
 from below, for they say that the
469𓀴            words used
 by Hugo Baskerville,
 when he was in wine, were
470𓀴            such
 as might blast the
 man who said them.  At last in the
471𓀴            stress of her fear she
 did that which might have daunted
472𓀴            the bravest or most
 active man, for by the aid of the
473𓀴            growth
 of ivy which covered
 (and still covers) the south
474𓀴            wall
 she came down from
 under the eaves, and so homeward
475𓀴            across the moor, there
 being three leagues betwixt the Hall
476𓀴            and her father’s farm.
477𓀴
478𓀴            “It chanced that some
 little time later Hugo left his
479𓀴            guests to carry food
 and drink—with other worse things,
480𓀴            perchance—to
 his captive,
 and so found the cage empty and
481𓀴            the bird
 escaped.  Then,
 as it would seem, he became as one
482𓀴            that
 hath a devil, for,
 rushing down the stairs into the
483𓀴            dining-hall, he sprang
 upon the great table, flagons and
484𓀴            trenchers
 flying before
 him, and he cried aloud before all
485𓀴            the company
 that he would
 that very night render his body
486𓀴            and soul to the Powers
 of Evil if he might but overtake the
487𓀴            wench.  And
 while the revellers
 stood aghast at the fury of
488𓀴            the man,
 one more wicked
 or, it may be, more drunken than
489𓀴            the rest,
 cried out that
 they should put the hounds upon
490𓀴            her.  Whereat Hugo ran
 from the house, crying to his grooms
491𓀴            that
 they should saddle
 his mare and unkennel the pack, and
492𓀴            giving
 the hounds a kerchief
 of the maid’s, he swung them
493𓀴            to the
 line, and so off
 full cry in the moonlight over the
494𓀴            moor.
495𓀴
496𓀴            “Now,
 for some space the
 revellers stood agape, unable to
497𓀴            understand
 all that had
 been done in such haste.  But anon
498𓀴            their
 bemused wits awoke
 to the nature of the deed which
499𓀴            was like
 to be done upon
 the moorlands.  Everything was now
500𓀴            in an
 uproar, some calling
 for their pistols, some for
501𓀴            their horses, and some
 for another flask of wine.  But at
502𓀴            length some sense came
 back to their crazed minds, and the
503𓀴            whole
 of them, thirteen
 in number, took horse and started
504𓀴            in pursuit.
  The moon shone
 clear above them, and they rode
505𓀴            swiftly
 abreast, taking
 that course which the maid must
506𓀴            needs have taken if
 she were to reach her own home.
507𓀴
508𓀴            “They had gone a mile
 or two when they passed one of the
509𓀴            night
 shepherds upon the
 moorlands, and they cried to him
510𓀴            to know if he had seen
 the hunt.  And the man, as the story
511𓀴            goes,
 was so crazed with
 fear that he could scarce speak,
512𓀴            but at
 last he said that
 he had indeed seen the unhappy
513𓀴            maiden,
 with the hounds
 upon her track.  ‘But I have seen
514𓀴            more than that,’ said
 he, ‘for Hugo Baskerville passed me
515𓀴            upon
 his black mare, and
 there ran mute behind him such a
516𓀴            hound of hell as God
 forbid should ever be at my heels.’
517𓀴            So the drunken squires
 cursed the shepherd and rode onward.
518𓀴             But soon their skins
 turned cold, for there came a
519𓀴            galloping
 across the moor,
 and the black mare, dabbled with
520𓀴            white froth, went past
 with trailing bridle and empty
521𓀴            saddle.
  Then the revellers
 rode close together, for a
522𓀴            great
 fear was on them,
 but they still followed over the
523𓀴            moor, though each, had
 he been alone, would have been right
524𓀴            glad
 to have turned his
 horse’s head.  Riding slowly in
525𓀴            this fashion they came
 at last upon the hounds.  These,
526𓀴            though known for their
 valour and their breed, were
527𓀴            whimpering
 in a cluster
 at the head of a deep dip or goyal,
528𓀴            as we
 call it, upon the
 moor, some slinking away and some,
529𓀴            with starting hackles
 and staring eyes, gazing down the
530𓀴            narrow
 valley before them.
531𓀴
532𓀴            “The
 company had come to
 a halt, more sober men, as you may
533𓀴            guess, than when they
 started.  The most of them would by
534𓀴            no means
 advance, but three
 of them, the boldest, or it may
535𓀴            be the
 most drunken, rode
 forward down the goyal. Now, it
536𓀴            opened
 into a broad space
 in which stood two of those great
537𓀴            stones,
 still to be seen
 there, which were set by certain
538𓀴            forgotten
 peoples in the
 days of old. The moon was shining
539𓀴            bright
 upon the clearing,
 and there in the centre lay the
540𓀴            unhappy maid where she
 had fallen, dead of fear and of
541𓀴            fatigue.
  But it was not
 the sight of her body, nor yet was
542𓀴            it that of the body of
 Hugo Baskerville lying near her,
543𓀴            which raised the hair
 upon the heads of these three
544𓀴            dare-devil roysterers,
 but it was that, standing over Hugo,
545𓀴            and plucking
 at his throat,
 there stood a foul thing, a
546𓀴            great,
 black beast, shaped
 like a hound, yet larger than
547𓀴            any hound
 that ever mortal
 eye has rested upon.  And even
548𓀴            as they looked the
 thing tore the throat out of Hugo
549𓀴            Baskerville, on which,
 as it turned its blazing eyes and
550𓀴            dripping
 jaws upon them,
 the three shrieked with fear and
551𓀴            rode for
 dear life, still
 screaming, across the moor.  One,
552𓀴            it is said, died that
 very night of what he had seen, and
553𓀴            the other
 twain were but
 broken men for the rest of their
554𓀴            days.
555𓀴
556𓀴            “Such is the tale, my
 sons, of the coming of the hound
557𓀴            which is said to have
 plagued the family so sorely ever
558𓀴            since.  If I have set
 it down it is because that which is
559𓀴            clearly
 known hath less
 terror than that which is but
560𓀴            hinted at and guessed.
  Nor can it be denied that many of
561𓀴            the family have been
 unhappy in their deaths, which have
562𓀴            been sudden, bloody,
 and mysterious.  Yet may we shelter
563𓀴            ourselves
 in the infinite
 goodness of Providence, which
564𓀴            would
 not forever punish
 the innocent beyond that third or
565𓀴            fourth
 generation which
 is threatened in Holy Writ.  To
566𓀴            that Providence, my
 sons, I hereby commend you, and I
567𓀴            counsel you by way of
 caution to forbear from crossing the
568𓀴            moor in those dark
 hours when the powers of evil are
569𓀴            exalted.
570𓀴
571𓀴            “[This
 from Hugo Baskerville
 to his sons Rodger and John,
572𓀴            with instructions that
 they say nothing thereof to their
573𓀴            sister Elizabeth.]”
574𓀴
575𓀴      When Dr. Mortimer
 had finished
 reading this singular narrative he
576𓀴      pushed his spectacles
 up on his
 forehead and stared across at Mr.
577𓀴      Sherlock Holmes. The latter
 yawned and tossed the end of his
578𓀴      cigarette into the fire.
579𓀴
580𓀴      “Well?” said he.
581𓀴
582𓀴      “Do you not find
 it interesting?”
583𓀴
584𓀴      “To a collector
 of fairy tales.”
585𓀴
586𓀴      Dr. Mortimer drew a folded
 newspaper out of his pocket.
587𓀴
588𓀴      “Now, Mr. Holmes, we will
 give you something a little more
589𓀴      recent. This
 is the _Devon County
 Chronicle_ of May 14th of this
590𓀴      year. It is
 a short account of
 the facts elicited at the death of
591𓀴      Sir Charles
 Baskerville which
 occurred a few days before that
592𓀴      date.”
593𓀴
594𓀴      My friend leaned a little
 forward and his expression became
595𓀴      intent. Our
 visitor readjusted
 his glasses and began:
596𓀴
597𓀴        “The recent
 sudden death of
 Sir Charles Baskerville, whose name
598𓀴        has been mentioned as the
 probable Liberal candidate for
599𓀴        Mid-Devon at
 the next election,
 has cast a gloom over the
600𓀴        county. 
 Though Sir Charles
 had resided at Baskerville Hall for
601𓀴        a comparatively
 short period
 his amiability of character and
602𓀴        extreme generosity had won
 the affection and respect of all who
603𓀴        had been brought
 into contact
 with him.  In these days of
604𓀴        _nouveaux riches_
 it is refreshing
 to find a case where the
605𓀴        scion of an
 old county family
 which has fallen upon evil days
606𓀴        is able to
 make his own fortune
 and to bring it back with him
607𓀴        to restore
 the fallen grandeur
 of his line.  Sir Charles, as is
608𓀴        well known, made large
 sums of money in South African
609𓀴        speculation.
 More wise than
 those who go on until the wheel
610𓀴        turns against
 them, he realised
 his gains and returned to
611𓀴        England with them.  It is
 only two years since he took up his
612𓀴        residence
 at Baskerville Hall,
 and it is common talk how large
613𓀴        were those schemes
 of reconstruction
 and improvement which have
614𓀴        been interrupted
 by his death.
  Being himself childless, it was
615𓀴        his openly
 expressed desire
 that the whole countryside should,
616𓀴        within his
 own lifetime, profit
 by his good fortune, and many
617𓀴        will have personal reasons
 for bewailing his untimely end.  His
618𓀴        generous
 donations to local
 and county charities have been
619𓀴        frequently chronicled
 in these columns.
620𓀴
621𓀴            “The circumstances
 connected
 with the death of Sir Charles
622𓀴            cannot be said to have
 been entirely cleared up by the
623𓀴            inquest, but at least
 enough has been done to dispose of
624𓀴            those rumours to which
 local superstition has given rise.
625𓀴            There is
 no reason whatever
 to suspect foul play, or to
626𓀴            imagine
 that death could
 be from any but natural causes.
627𓀴            Sir Charles
 was a widower,
 and a man who may be said to
628𓀴            have been in some ways
 of an eccentric habit of mind. In
629𓀴            spite
 of his considerable
 wealth he was simple in his
630𓀴            personal tastes, and
 his indoor servants at Baskerville
631𓀴            Hall consisted
 of a married
 couple named Barrymore, the
632𓀴            husband
 acting as butler
 and the wife as housekeeper. Their
633𓀴            evidence, corroborated
 by that of several friends, tends to
634𓀴            show
 that Sir Charles’s
 health has for some time been
635𓀴            impaired, and points
 especially to some affection of the
636𓀴            heart, manifesting
 itself in changes of colour,
637𓀴            breathlessness,
 and acute
 attacks of nervous depression.
638𓀴            Dr. James
 Mortimer, the
 friend and medical attendant of the
639𓀴            deceased, has given
 evidence to the same effect.
640𓀴
641𓀴            “The facts of the case
 are simple.  Sir Charles Baskerville
642𓀴            was in the habit every
 night before going to bed of walking
643𓀴            down the famous yew
 alley of Baskerville Hall.  The
644𓀴            evidence
 of the Barrymores
 shows that this had been his
645𓀴            custom. On the fourth
 of May Sir Charles had declared his
646𓀴            intention of starting
 next day for London, and had ordered
647𓀴            Barrymore
 to prepare his
 luggage.  That night he went out
648𓀴            as usual
 for his nocturnal
 walk, in the course of which he
649𓀴            was in
 the habit of smoking
 a cigar.  He never returned.
650𓀴            At twelve
 o’clock Barrymore,
 finding the hall door still
651𓀴            open, became alarmed,
 and, lighting a lantern, went in
652𓀴            search of his master.
  The day had been wet, and Sir
653𓀴            Charles’s footmarks
 were easily traced down the alley.
654𓀴            Halfway down this walk
 there is a gate which leads out on
655𓀴            to the
 moor. There were
 indications that Sir Charles had
656𓀴            stood for some little
 time here.  He then proceeded down
657𓀴            the alley, and it was
 at the far end of it that his body
658𓀴            was discovered.
 One fact
 which has not been explained is
659𓀴            the statement
 of Barrymore
 that his master’s footprints
660𓀴            altered
 their character
 from the time that he passed the
661𓀴            moor-gate, and that he
 appeared from thence onward to have
662𓀴            been walking upon
 his toes.  One Murphy, a gipsy
663𓀴            horse-dealer,
 was on the
 moor at no great distance at the
664𓀴            time,
 but he appears by
 his own confession to have been the
665𓀴            worse for
 drink. He declares
 that he heard cries but is
666𓀴            unable
 to state from what
 direction they came.  No signs of
667𓀴            violence
 were to be discovered
 upon Sir Charles’s person,
668𓀴            and though
 the doctor’s
 evidence pointed to an almost
669𓀴            incredible
 facial distortion—so
 great that Dr. Mortimer
670𓀴            refused
 at first to believe
 that it was indeed his friend
671𓀴            and patient
 who lay before
 him—it was explained that that
672𓀴            is a symptom which is
 not unusual in cases of dyspnœa and
673𓀴            death from
 cardiac exhaustion.
  This explanation was borne
674𓀴            out by the post-mortem
 examination, which showed
675𓀴            long-standing organic
 disease, and the coroner’s jury
676𓀴            returned
 a verdict in accordance
 with the medical evidence.
677𓀴             It is well that this
 is so, for it is obviously of the
678𓀴            utmost importance that
 Sir Charles’s heir should settle at
679𓀴            the Hall
 and continue the
 good work which has been so sadly
680𓀴            interrupted.  Had the
 prosaic finding of the coroner not
681𓀴            finally put an end to
 the romantic stories which have been
682𓀴            whispered
 in connection
 with the affair, it might have been
683𓀴            difficult
 to find a tenant
 for Baskerville Hall.  It is
684𓀴            understood
 that the next
 of kin is Mr. Henry Baskerville,
685𓀴            if he be still alive,
 the son of Sir Charles Baskerville’s
686𓀴            younger brother.  The
 young man when last heard of was in
687𓀴            America, and inquiries
 are being instituted with a view to
688𓀴            informing him
 of his good fortune.”
689𓀴
690𓀴      Dr. Mortimer
 refolded his paper
 and replaced it in his pocket.
691𓀴      “Those are the public facts,
 Mr. Holmes, in connection with the
692𓀴      death of Sir
 Charles Baskerville.”
693𓀴
694𓀴      “I must thank you,” said
 Sherlock Holmes, “for calling my
695𓀴      attention to a
 case which certainly
 presents some features of
696𓀴      interest. I
 had observed some
 newspaper comment at the time, but
697𓀴      I was exceedingly
 preoccupied
 by that little affair of the
698𓀴      Vatican cameos,
 and in my anxiety
 to oblige the Pope I lost touch
699𓀴      with several
 interesting English
 cases. This article, you say,
700𓀴      contains all
 the public facts?”
701𓀴
702𓀴      “It does.”
703𓀴
704𓀴      “Then let me
 have the private
 ones.” He leaned back, put his
705𓀴      finger-tips together,
 and assumed
 his most impassive and judicial
706𓀴      expression.
707𓀴
708𓀴      “In doing so,”
 said Dr. Mortimer,
 who had begun to show signs of
709𓀴      some strong emotion,
 “I am telling
 that which I have not confided
710𓀴      to anyone. My motive for
 withholding it from the coroner’s
711𓀴      inquiry is that
 a man of science
 shrinks from placing himself in
712𓀴      the public
 position of seeming
 to indorse a popular superstition.
713𓀴      I had the further
 motive that
 Baskerville Hall, as the paper
714𓀴      says, would certainly remain
 untenanted if anything were done to
715𓀴      increase its already rather
 grim reputation. For both these
716𓀴      reasons I thought that I was
 justified in telling rather less
717𓀴      than I knew,
 since no practical
 good could result from it, but
718𓀴      with you there is no reason
 why I should not be perfectly frank.
719𓀴
720𓀴      “The moor is very sparsely
 inhabited, and those who live near
721𓀴      each other are
 thrown very much
 together. For this reason I saw a
722𓀴      good deal of Sir
 Charles Baskerville.
 With the exception of Mr.
723𓀴      Frankland, of Lafter Hall,
 and Mr. Stapleton, the naturalist,
724𓀴      there are no other men of
 education within many miles. Sir
725𓀴      Charles was
 a retiring man, but
 the chance of his illness brought
726𓀴      us together, and a community
 of interests in science kept us so.
727𓀴      He had brought back much
 scientific information from South
728𓀴      Africa, and many a charming
 evening we have spent together
729𓀴      discussing the comparative
 anatomy of the Bushman and the
730𓀴      Hottentot.
731𓀴
732𓀴      “Within the last few months
 it became increasingly plain to me
733𓀴      that Sir Charles’s nervous
 system was strained to the breaking
734𓀴      point. He had
 taken this legend
 which I have read you exceedingly
735𓀴      to heart—so much so that,
 although he would walk in his own
736𓀴      grounds, nothing
 would induce
 him to go out upon the moor at
737𓀴      night. Incredible as it may
 appear to you, Mr. Holmes, he was
738𓀴      honestly convinced
 that a dreadful
 fate overhung his family, and
739𓀴      certainly the records which
 he was able to give of his ancestors
740𓀴      were not encouraging. The
 idea of some ghastly presence
741𓀴      constantly haunted him, and
 on more than one occasion he has
742𓀴      asked me whether I had on my
 medical journeys at night ever seen
743𓀴      any strange
 creature or heard
 the baying of a hound. The latter
744𓀴      question he
 put to me several
 times, and always with a voice
745𓀴      which vibrated
 with excitement.
746𓀴
747𓀴      “I can well remember driving
 up to his house in the evening some
748𓀴      three weeks before the fatal
 event. He chanced to be at his hall
749𓀴      door. I had descended from
 my gig and was standing in front of
750𓀴      him, when I saw his eyes fix
 themselves over my shoulder and
751𓀴      stare past me
 with an expression
 of the most dreadful horror. I
752𓀴      whisked round
 and had just time
 to catch a glimpse of something
753𓀴      which I took
 to be a large black
 calf passing at the head of the
754𓀴      drive. So excited
 and alarmed
 was he that I was compelled to go
755𓀴      down to the spot where the
 animal had been and look around for
756𓀴      it. It was
 gone, however, and
 the incident appeared to make the
757𓀴      worst impression upon his
 mind. I stayed with him all the
758𓀴      evening, and it was on that
 occasion, to explain the emotion
759𓀴      which he had shown,
 that he confided
 to my keeping that narrative
760𓀴      which I read
 to you when first
 I came. I mention this small
761𓀴      episode because
 it assumes some
 importance in view of the tragedy
762𓀴      which followed,
 but I was convinced
 at the time that the matter
763𓀴      was entirely trivial and
 that his excitement had no
764𓀴      justification.
765𓀴
766𓀴      “It was at
 my advice that Sir
 Charles was about to go to London.
767𓀴      His heart was,
 I knew, affected,
 and the constant anxiety in
768𓀴      which he lived,
 however chimerical
 the cause of it might be, was
769𓀴      evidently having
 a serious effect
 upon his health. I thought that
770𓀴      a few months among
 the distractions
 of town would send him back a
771𓀴      new man. Mr.
 Stapleton, a mutual
 friend who was much concerned at
772𓀴      his state of
 health, was of the
 same opinion. At the last instant
773𓀴      came this terrible
 catastrophe.
774𓀴
775𓀴      “On the night
 of Sir Charles’s
 death Barrymore the butler, who
776𓀴      made the discovery,
 sent Perkins
 the groom on horseback to me,
777𓀴      and as I was sitting up late
 I was able to reach Baskerville Hall
778𓀴      within an hour of the event.
 I checked and corroborated all the
779𓀴      facts which were mentioned
 at the inquest. I followed the
780𓀴      footsteps down
 the yew alley,
 I saw the spot at the moor-gate
781𓀴      where he seemed
 to have waited,
 I remarked the change in the
782𓀴      shape of the
 prints after that
 point, I noted that there were no
783𓀴      other footsteps
 save those of
 Barrymore on the soft gravel, and
784𓀴      finally I carefully examined
 the body, which had not been touched
785𓀴      until my arrival.
 Sir Charles
 lay on his face, his arms out, his
786𓀴      fingers dug into the ground,
 and his features convulsed with some
787𓀴      strong emotion
 to such an extent
 that I could hardly have sworn
788𓀴      to his identity. There was
 certainly no physical injury of any
789𓀴      kind. But one
 false statement
 was made by Barrymore at the
790𓀴      inquest. He said that there
 were no traces upon the ground round
791𓀴      the body. He did not observe
 any. But I did—some little distance
792𓀴      off, but fresh and clear.”
793𓀴
794𓀴      “Footprints?”
795𓀴
796𓀴      “Footprints.”
797𓀴
798𓀴      “A man’s or a woman’s?”
799𓀴
800𓀴      Dr. Mortimer
 looked strangely
 at us for an instant, and his voice
801𓀴      sank almost to a
 whisper as he answered.
802𓀴
803𓀴      “Mr. Holmes, they were the
 footprints of a gigantic hound!”
804𓀴
805𓀴
806𓀴
807𓀴
808𓀴Chapter 3.
809𓀴The Problem
810𓀴
811𓀴
812𓀴      I confess at
 these words a shudder
 passed through me. There was a
813𓀴      thrill in the doctor’s voice
 which showed that he was himself
814𓀴      deeply moved
 by that which he
 told us. Holmes leaned forward in
815𓀴      his excitement and his eyes
 had the hard, dry glitter which shot
816𓀴      from them when he
 was keenly interested.
817𓀴
818𓀴      “You saw this?”
819𓀴
820𓀴      “As clearly as I see you.”
821𓀴
822𓀴      “And you said nothing?”
823𓀴
824𓀴      “What was the use?”
825𓀴
826𓀴      “How was it that
 no one else saw it?”
827𓀴
828𓀴      “The marks were some twenty
 yards from the body and no one gave
829𓀴      them a thought.
 I don’t suppose
 I should have done so had I not
830𓀴      known this legend.”
831𓀴
832𓀴      “There are many
 sheep-dogs on the moor?”
833𓀴
834𓀴      “No doubt, but
 this was no sheep-dog.”
835𓀴
836𓀴      “You say it was large?”
837𓀴
838𓀴      “Enormous.”
839𓀴
840𓀴      “But it had not
 approached the body?”
841𓀴
842𓀴      “No.”
843𓀴
844𓀴      “What sort of night was it?’
845𓀴
846𓀴      “Damp and raw.”
847𓀴
848𓀴      “But not actually raining?”
849𓀴
850𓀴      “No.”
851𓀴
852𓀴      “What is the alley like?”
853𓀴
854𓀴      “There are two lines of old
 yew hedge, twelve feet high and
855𓀴      impenetrable.
 The walk in the
 centre is about eight feet across.”
856𓀴
857𓀴      “Is there anything between
 the hedges and the walk?”
858𓀴
859𓀴      “Yes, there
 is a strip of grass
 about six feet broad on either
860𓀴      side.”
861𓀴
862𓀴      “I understand
 that the yew hedge
 is penetrated at one point by a
863𓀴      gate?”
864𓀴
865𓀴      “Yes, the wicket-gate
 which leads on to the moor.”
866𓀴
867𓀴      “Is there any
 other opening?”
868𓀴
869𓀴      “None.”
870𓀴
871𓀴      “So that to
 reach the yew alley
 one either has to come down it
872𓀴      from the house or else
 to enter it by the moor-gate?”
873𓀴
874𓀴      “There is an exit through
 a summer-house at the far end.”
875𓀴
876𓀴      “Had Sir Charles
 reached this?”
877𓀴
878𓀴      “No; he lay about
 fifty yards from it.”
879𓀴
880𓀴      “Now, tell me,
 Dr. Mortimer—and
 this is important—the marks which
881𓀴      you saw were on the
 path and not on the grass?”
882𓀴
883𓀴      “No marks could
 show on the grass.”
884𓀴
885𓀴      “Were they on the same side
 of the path as the moor-gate?”
886𓀴
887𓀴      “Yes; they were on the edge
 of the path on the same side as the
888𓀴      moor-gate.”
889𓀴
890𓀴      “You interest
 me exceedingly.
 Another point. Was the wicket-gate
891𓀴      closed?”
892𓀴
893𓀴      “Closed and padlocked.”
894𓀴
895𓀴      “How high was it?”
896𓀴
897𓀴      “About four feet high.”
898𓀴
899𓀴      “Then anyone could
 have got over it?”
900𓀴
901𓀴      “Yes.”
902𓀴
903𓀴      “And what marks did
 you see by the wicket-gate?”
904𓀴
905𓀴      “None in particular.”
906𓀴
907𓀴      “Good heaven!
 Did no one examine?”
908𓀴
909𓀴      “Yes, I examined, myself.”
910𓀴
911𓀴      “And found nothing?”
912𓀴
913𓀴      “It was all
 very confused. Sir
 Charles had evidently stood there
914𓀴      for five or ten minutes.”
915𓀴
916𓀴      “How do you know that?”
917𓀴
918𓀴      “Because the ash had twice
 dropped from his cigar.”
919𓀴
920𓀴      “Excellent!
 This is a colleague,
 Watson, after our own heart. But
921𓀴      the marks?”
922𓀴
923𓀴      “He had left
 his own marks all
 over that small patch of gravel. I
924𓀴      could discern no others.”
925𓀴
926𓀴      Sherlock Holmes struck his
 hand against his knee with an
927𓀴      impatient gesture.
928𓀴
929𓀴      “If I had only been there!”
 he cried. “It is evidently a case of
930𓀴      extraordinary interest,
 and one which presented immense
931𓀴      opportunities
 to the scientific
 expert. That gravel page upon
932𓀴      which I might
 have read so much
 has been long ere this smudged by
933𓀴      the rain and defaced by the
 clogs of curious peasants. Oh, Dr.
934𓀴      Mortimer, Dr.
 Mortimer, to think
 that you should not have called
935𓀴      me in! You have indeed
 much to answer for.”
936𓀴
937𓀴      “I could not
 call you in, Mr.
 Holmes, without disclosing these
938𓀴      facts to the
 world, and I have
 already given my reasons for not
939𓀴      wishing to do
 so. Besides, besides—”
940𓀴
941𓀴      “Why do you hesitate?”
942𓀴
943𓀴      “There is a
 realm in which the
 most acute and most experienced of
944𓀴      detectives is helpless.”
945𓀴
946𓀴      “You mean that the
 thing is supernatural?”
947𓀴
948𓀴      “I did not
 positively say so.”
949𓀴
950𓀴      “No, but you
 evidently think it.”
951𓀴
952𓀴      “Since the tragedy,
 Mr. Holmes,
 there have come to my ears
953𓀴      several incidents which are
 hard to reconcile with the settled
954𓀴      order of Nature.”
955𓀴
956𓀴      “For example?”
957𓀴
958𓀴      “I find that
 before the terrible
 event occurred several people
959𓀴      had seen a creature upon the
 moor which corresponds with this
960𓀴      Baskerville demon, and which
 could not possibly be any animal
961𓀴      known to science. They all
 agreed that it was a huge creature,
962𓀴      luminous, ghastly,
 and spectral.
 I have cross-examined these men,
963𓀴      one of them a hard-headed
 countryman,
 one a farrier, and one a
964𓀴      moorland farmer,
 who all tell
 the same story of this dreadful
965𓀴      apparition, exactly
 corresponding
 to the hell-hound of the
966𓀴      legend. I assure you that
 there is a reign of terror in the
967𓀴      district, and
 that it is a hardy
 man who will cross the moor at
968𓀴      night.”
969𓀴
970𓀴      “And you, a trained man
 of science, believe it to be
971𓀴      supernatural?”
972𓀴
973𓀴      “I do not know
 what to believe.”
974𓀴
975𓀴      Holmes shrugged
 his shoulders.
 “I have hitherto confined my
976𓀴      investigations
 to this world,”
 said he. “In a modest way I have
977𓀴      combated evil,
 but to take on
 the Father of Evil himself would,
978𓀴      perhaps, be too ambitious a
 task. Yet you must admit that the
979𓀴      footmark is material.”
980𓀴
981𓀴      “The original
 hound was material
 enough to tug a man’s throat
982𓀴      out, and yet he
 was diabolical as well.”
983𓀴
984𓀴      “I see that
 you have quite gone
 over to the supernaturalists. But
985𓀴      now, Dr. Mortimer, tell me
 this. If you hold these views, why
986𓀴      have you come to consult me
 at all? You tell me in the same
987𓀴      breath that it is useless to
 investigate Sir Charles’s death, and
988𓀴      that you desire
 me to do it.”
989𓀴
990𓀴      “I did not say that
 I desired you to do it.”
991𓀴
992𓀴      “Then, how
 can I assist you?”
993𓀴
994𓀴      “By advising me as to what
 I should do with Sir Henry
995𓀴      Baskerville, who
 arrives at Waterloo
 Station”—Dr. Mortimer looked
996𓀴      at his watch—“in exactly
 one hour and a quarter.”
997𓀴
998𓀴      “He being the heir?”
999𓀴
1000𓀴      “Yes. On the death of Sir
 Charles we inquired for this young
1001𓀴      gentleman and found that he
 had been farming in Canada. From the
1002𓀴      accounts which have reached
 us he is an excellent fellow in every
1003𓀴      way. I speak now not as a
 medical man but as a trustee and
1004𓀴      executor of
 Sir Charles’s will.”
1005𓀴
1006𓀴      “There is no other
 claimant, I presume?”
1007𓀴
1008𓀴      “None. The
 only other kinsman
 whom we have been able to trace was
1009𓀴      Rodger Baskerville,
 the youngest
 of three brothers of whom poor
1010𓀴      Sir Charles
 was the elder. The
 second brother, who died young, is
1011𓀴      the father
 of this lad Henry.
 The third, Rodger, was the black
1012𓀴      sheep of the
 family. He came
 of the old masterful Baskerville
1013𓀴      strain and
 was the very image,
 they tell me, of the family
1014𓀴      picture of
 old Hugo. He made
 England too hot to hold him, fled to
1015𓀴      Central America,
 and died there
 in 1876 of yellow fever. Henry is
1016𓀴      the last of
 the Baskervilles.
 In one hour and five minutes I meet
1017𓀴      him at Waterloo Station. I
 have had a wire that he arrived at
1018𓀴      Southampton
 this morning. Now,
 Mr. Holmes, what would you advise
1019𓀴      me to do with him?”
1020𓀴
1021𓀴      “Why should he not go
 to the home of his fathers?”
1022𓀴
1023𓀴      “It seems natural, does it
 not? And yet, consider that every
1024𓀴      Baskerville who goes there
 meets with an evil fate. I feel sure
1025𓀴      that if Sir
 Charles could have
 spoken with me before his death he
1026𓀴      would have
 warned me against
 bringing this, the last of the old
1027𓀴      race, and the heir to great
 wealth, to that deadly place. And yet
1028𓀴      it cannot
 be denied that the
 prosperity of the whole poor, bleak
1029𓀴      countryside
 depends upon his
 presence. All the good work which
1030𓀴      has been done
 by Sir Charles
 will crash to the ground if there is
1031𓀴      no tenant
 of the Hall. I fear
 lest I should be swayed too much by
1032𓀴      my own obvious interest in
 the matter, and that is why I bring
1033𓀴      the case before you
 and ask for your advice.”
1034𓀴
1035𓀴      Holmes considered
 for a little time.
1036𓀴
1037𓀴      “Put into plain words, the
 matter is this,” said he. “In your
1038𓀴      opinion there
 is a diabolical
 agency which makes Dartmoor an
1039𓀴      unsafe abode
 for a Baskerville—that
 is your opinion?”
1040𓀴
1041𓀴      “At least I
 might go the length
 of saying that there is some
1042𓀴      evidence that
 this may be so.”
1043𓀴
1044𓀴      “Exactly.
 But surely, if your
 supernatural theory be correct, it
1045𓀴      could work the young man
 evil in London as easily as in
1046𓀴      Devonshire.
 A devil with merely
 local powers like a parish vestry
1047𓀴      would be too inconceivable
 a thing.”
1048𓀴
1049𓀴      “You put the matter
 more flippantly,
 Mr. Holmes, than you would
1050𓀴      probably do
 if you were brought
 into personal contact with these
1051𓀴      things. Your
 advice, then, as
 I understand it, is that the young
1052𓀴      man will be
 as safe in Devonshire
 as in London. He comes in fifty
1053𓀴      minutes. What
 would you recommend?”
1054𓀴
1055𓀴      “I recommend, sir, that you
 take a cab, call off your spaniel who
1056𓀴      is scratching
 at my front door,
 and proceed to Waterloo to meet
1057𓀴      Sir Henry Baskerville.”
1058𓀴
1059𓀴      “And then?”
1060𓀴
1061𓀴      “And then you
 will say nothing
 to him at all until I have made up
1062𓀴      my mind about the matter.”
1063𓀴
1064𓀴      “How long will it take
 you to make up your mind?”
1065𓀴
1066𓀴      “Twenty-four
 hours. At ten o’clock
 tomorrow, Dr. Mortimer, I will
1067𓀴      be much obliged
 to you if you
 will call upon me here, and it will
1068𓀴      be of help
 to me in my plans
 for the future if you will bring Sir
1069𓀴      Henry Baskerville
 with you.”
1070𓀴
1071𓀴      “I will do so, Mr. Holmes.”
 He scribbled the appointment on his
1072𓀴      shirt-cuff
 and hurried off in
 his strange, peering, absent-minded
1073𓀴      fashion. Holmes stopped
 him at the head of the stair.
1074𓀴
1075𓀴      “Only one
 more question, Dr.
 Mortimer. You say that before Sir
1076𓀴      Charles Baskerville’s death
 several people saw this apparition
1077𓀴      upon the moor?”
1078𓀴
1079𓀴      “Three people did.”
1080𓀴
1081𓀴      “Did any see it after?”
1082𓀴
1083𓀴      “I have not heard of any.”
1084𓀴
1085𓀴      “Thank you. Good-morning.”
1086𓀴
1087𓀴      Holmes returned to his seat
 with that quiet look of inward
1088𓀴      satisfaction
 which meant that
 he had a congenial task before him.
1089𓀴
1090𓀴      “Going out, Watson?”
1091𓀴
1092𓀴      “Unless I can help you.”
1093𓀴
1094𓀴      “No, my dear fellow, it is
 at the hour of action that I turn to
1095𓀴      you for aid.
 But this is splendid,
 really unique from some points
1096𓀴      of view. When
 you pass Bradley’s,
 would you ask him to send up a
1097𓀴      pound of the strongest shag
 tobacco? Thank you. It would be as
1098𓀴      well if you could make it
 convenient not to return before
1099𓀴      evening. Then
 I should be very
 glad to compare impressions as to
1100𓀴      this most
 interesting problem
 which has been submitted to us this
1101𓀴      morning.”
1102𓀴
1103𓀴      I knew that seclusion and
 solitude were very necessary for my
1104𓀴      friend in those
 hours of intense
 mental concentration during
1105𓀴      which he weighed
 every particle
 of evidence, constructed
1106𓀴      alternative
 theories, balanced
 one against the other, and made up
1107𓀴      his mind as to which points
 were essential and which immaterial.
1108𓀴      I therefore
 spent the day at
 my club and did not return to Baker
1109𓀴      Street until
 evening. It was
 nearly nine o’clock when I found
1110𓀴      myself in the
 sitting-room once more.
1111𓀴
1112𓀴      My first impression
 as I opened
 the door was that a fire had
1113𓀴      broken out,
 for the room was
 so filled with smoke that the light
1114𓀴      of the lamp upon the table
 was blurred by it. As I entered,
1115𓀴      however, my
 fears were set at
 rest, for it was the acrid fumes of
1116𓀴      strong coarse tobacco which
 took me by the throat and set me
1117𓀴      coughing.
 Through the haze I
 had a vague vision of Holmes in his
1118𓀴      dressing-gown coiled up in
 an armchair with his black clay pipe
1119𓀴      between his lips. Several
 rolls of paper lay around him.
1120𓀴
1121𓀴      “Caught cold,
 Watson?” said he.
1122𓀴
1123𓀴      “No, it’s this
 poisonous atmosphere.”
1124𓀴
1125𓀴      “I suppose it _is_ pretty
 thick, now that you mention it.”
1126𓀴
1127𓀴      “Thick! It is intolerable.”
1128𓀴
1129𓀴      “Open the window, then! You
 have been at your club all day, I
1130𓀴      perceive.”
1131𓀴
1132𓀴      “My dear Holmes!”
1133𓀴
1134𓀴      “Am I right?”
1135𓀴
1136𓀴      “Certainly, but how?”
1137𓀴
1138𓀴      He laughed at my bewildered
 expression. “There is a delightful
1139𓀴      freshness
 about you, Watson,
 which makes it a pleasure to
1140𓀴      exercise any small powers
 which I possess at your expense. A
1141𓀴      gentleman goes forth on a
 showery and miry day. He returns
1142𓀴      immaculate
 in the evening with
 the gloss still on his hat and his
1143𓀴      boots. He
 has been a fixture
 therefore all day. He is not a man
1144𓀴      with intimate
 friends. Where,
 then, could he have been? Is it not
1145𓀴      obvious?”
1146𓀴
1147𓀴      “Well, it
 is rather obvious.”
1148𓀴
1149𓀴      “The world
 is full of obvious
 things which nobody by any chance
1150𓀴      ever observes. Where do
 you think that I have been?”
1151𓀴
1152𓀴      “A fixture also.”
1153𓀴
1154𓀴      “On the contrary,
 I have been to Devonshire.”
1155𓀴
1156𓀴      “In spirit?”
1157𓀴
1158𓀴      “Exactly. My
 body has remained
 in this armchair and has, I regret
1159𓀴      to observe, consumed in my
 absence two large pots of coffee and
1160𓀴      an incredible
 amount of tobacco.
 After you left I sent down to
1161𓀴      Stamford’s for the Ordnance
 map of this portion of the moor, and
1162𓀴      my spirit has hovered over
 it all day. I flatter myself that I
1163𓀴      could find my way about.”
1164𓀴
1165𓀴      “A large-scale
 map, I presume?”
1166𓀴
1167𓀴      “Very large.”
1168𓀴
1169𓀴      He unrolled one section and
 held it over his knee. “Here you have
1170𓀴      the particular
 district which
 concerns us. That is Baskerville
1171𓀴      Hall in the middle.”
1172𓀴
1173𓀴      “With a wood round it?”
1174𓀴
1175𓀴      “Exactly. I fancy the yew
 alley, though not marked under that
1176𓀴      name, must stretch along
 this line, with the moor, as you
1177𓀴      perceive, upon the right of
 it. This small clump of buildings
1178𓀴      here is the
 hamlet of Grimpen,
 where our friend Dr. Mortimer has
1179𓀴      his headquarters.
 Within a radius
 of five miles there are, as you
1180𓀴      see, only a
 very few scattered
 dwellings. Here is Lafter Hall,
1181𓀴      which was mentioned
 in the narrative.
 There is a house indicated
1182𓀴      here which
 may be the residence
 of the naturalist—Stapleton, if I
1183𓀴      remember right,
 was his name.
 Here are two moorland farmhouses,
1184𓀴      High Tor and Foulmire. Then
 fourteen miles away the great convict
1185𓀴      prison of
 Princetown. Between
 and around these scattered points
1186𓀴      extends the
 desolate, lifeless
 moor. This, then, is the stage
1187𓀴      upon which tragedy has been
 played, and upon which we may help to
1188𓀴      play it again.”
1189𓀴
1190𓀴      “It must be a wild place.”
1191𓀴
1192𓀴      “Yes, the
 setting is a worthy
 one. If the devil did desire to
1193𓀴      have a hand in
 the affairs of men—”
1194𓀴
1195𓀴      “Then you are yourself
 inclining to the supernatural
1196𓀴      explanation.”
1197𓀴
1198𓀴      “The devil’s agents may be
 of flesh and blood, may they not?
1199𓀴      There are two
 questions waiting
 for us at the outset. The one is
1200𓀴      whether any crime
 has been committed
 at all; the second is, what
1201𓀴      is the crime and how was
 it committed? Of course, if Dr.
1202𓀴      Mortimer’s surmise should
 be correct, and we are dealing with
1203𓀴      forces outside the ordinary
 laws of Nature, there is an end of
1204𓀴      our investigation. But we
 are bound to exhaust all other
1205𓀴      hypotheses
 before falling back
 upon this one. I think we’ll shut
1206𓀴      that window
 again, if you don’t
 mind. It is a singular thing, but
1207𓀴      I find that a concentrated
 atmosphere helps a concentration of
1208𓀴      thought. I
 have not pushed it
 to the length of getting into a box
1209𓀴      to think, but
 that is the logical
 outcome of my convictions. Have
1210𓀴      you turned the
 case over in your mind?”
1211𓀴
1212𓀴      “Yes, I have thought a good
 deal of it in the course of the day.”
1213𓀴
1214𓀴      “What do you make of it?”
1215𓀴
1216𓀴      “It is very bewildering.”
1217𓀴
1218𓀴      “It has certainly
 a character
 of its own. There are points of
1219𓀴      distinction
 about it. That change
 in the footprints, for example.
1220𓀴      What do you make of that?”
1221𓀴
1222𓀴      “Mortimer said that the man
 had walked on tiptoe down that
1223𓀴      portion of the alley.”
1224𓀴
1225𓀴      “He only repeated what some
 fool had said at the inquest. Why
1226𓀴      should a man walk
 on tiptoe down the alley?”
1227𓀴
1228𓀴      “What then?”
1229𓀴
1230𓀴      “He was running,
 Watson—running
 desperately, running for his
1231𓀴      life, running
 until he burst
 his heart—and fell dead upon his
1232𓀴      face.”
1233𓀴
1234𓀴      “Running from what?”
1235𓀴
1236𓀴      “There lies
 our problem. There
 are indications that the man was
1237𓀴      crazed with fear before
 ever he began to run.”
1238𓀴
1239𓀴      “How can you say that?”
1240𓀴
1241𓀴      “I am presuming
 that the cause
 of his fears came to him across
1242𓀴      the moor. If
 that were so, and
 it seems most probable, only a man
1243𓀴      who had lost his wits would
 have run _from_ the house instead of
1244𓀴      towards it. If
 the gipsy’s evidence
 may be taken as true, he ran
1245𓀴      with cries for
 help in the direction
 where help was least likely
1246𓀴      to be. Then,
 again, whom was
 he waiting for that night, and why
1247𓀴      was he waiting
 for him in the
 yew alley rather than in his own
1248𓀴      house?”
1249𓀴
1250𓀴      “You think that he
 was waiting for someone?”
1251𓀴
1252𓀴      “The man was
 elderly and infirm.
 We can understand his taking an
1253𓀴      evening stroll,
 but the ground
 was damp and the night inclement.
1254𓀴      Is it natural
 that he should
 stand for five or ten minutes, as
1255𓀴      Dr. Mortimer,
 with more practical
 sense than I should have given
1256𓀴      him credit for, deduced
 from the cigar ash?”
1257𓀴
1258𓀴      “But he went
 out every evening.”
1259𓀴

1825𓀴Chapter 5.
1826𓀴Three Broken Threads
1827𓀴

1831𓀴      which we had been involved

1833𓀴      He would talk

1836𓀴

1844𓀴

1855𓀴

2490𓀴      through deep lanes worn by

2491𓀴      either side,

2494𓀴      narrow granite bridge and

2495𓀴      swiftly down,

2498𓀴      delight, looking eagerly

2499𓀴      questions. To

2501𓀴      mark of the

2502𓀴      fluttered down upon us as

2511𓀴      equestrian statue

2519𓀴      warders watch

2525𓀴

2527𓀴      compared to the chance of

2536𓀴      taken an interest

2546𓀴      him out. It needed but this

2568𓀴      a maze of fantastic tracery

2571𓀴      black granite

 he asked in a low voice.
2582𓀴
2583𓀴      “No, no, the yew alley

2584𓀴

2586𓀴

2589𓀴      I’ll have a row of electric

2590𓀴      and you won’t

2591𓀴      and Edison right here

2594𓀴      before us.
 In the fading light

2595𓀴      heavy block

2599𓀴      crenelated,

2600𓀴      the turrets

2643𓀴      well-trained

2644𓀴      handsome,

2646𓀴
2647𓀴      “Would you wish dinner

2648𓀴
2649𓀴      “Is it ready?”
2650𓀴
2651𓀴      “In a very

 You will find hot water in your
2652𓀴      rooms. My

2654𓀴      understand

2660𓀴      we were able

2667𓀴

2669𓀴      they not? I should be sorry

2714𓀴      suppose one can tone down

2739𓀴      every nerve

2741𓀴
2742𓀴
2743𓀴
2744𓀴
2745𓀴Chapter 7.
2746𓀴The Stapletons of Merripit House
2747𓀴
2748𓀴
2749𓀴      The fresh beauty

2750𓀴      from our minds the grim and

2751𓀴      upon both

2752𓀴      Sir Henry and

2756𓀴      that this was

2757𓀴      into our souls

2758𓀴

2761𓀴      chilled by our drive, so we

2763𓀴

2776𓀴      Barrymore whether

2777𓀴      to me that

2844𓀴      Barrymore had

2848𓀴      he some sinister design of

2853𓀴      was that which

2859𓀴      complex case had come to

3254𓀴
3255𓀴      “We have books, we have our

3256𓀴      neighbours. Dr. Mortimer is

3257𓀴      Poor Sir Charles was also

3258𓀴      well and miss him more than

3259𓀴      should intrude if I were to

3260𓀴      acquaintance of Sir Henry?”
3261𓀴
3262𓀴      “I am sure that

3263𓀴
3264𓀴      “Then perhaps

3265𓀴      in our humble

3268𓀴      Lepidoptera? I think it

3269𓀴      south-west

3274𓀴      had been associated

3295𓀴

3297𓀴      Henry’s friend,

3298𓀴      Tell me why it was that you

3299𓀴      return to London.”
3300𓀴

3302𓀴      understand

3303𓀴      do.”
3304𓀴

3307𓀴      for ever since

3308𓀴      all round

3309𓀴      little green

3310𓀴      no guide to

3311𓀴      meant, and

3312𓀴

3325𓀴

3327𓀴
3328𓀴      “You know the

3329𓀴

3331𓀴

3333𓀴      away from a place which has

3335𓀴      danger?”
3336𓀴

3358𓀴
3359𓀴
3360𓀴
3361𓀴
3362𓀴Chapter 8.
3363𓀴First Report of Dr. Watson
3364𓀴
3365𓀴
3366𓀴      From this

 follow the course of events by
3367𓀴      transcribing my own letters

3368𓀴      before me on the table. One

3369𓀴      are exactly

3371𓀴      tragic events,

3372𓀴
3373𓀴      Baskerville
 Hall, October 13th.
3374𓀴
3375𓀴      MY DEAR HOLMES,
3376𓀴
3377𓀴      My previous
 letters and telegrams
 have kept you pretty well up to
3378𓀴      date as to all
 that has occurred

3379𓀴      of the world.
 The longer one
 stays here the more does the spirit
3380𓀴      of the moor sink into one’s
 soul, its vastness, and also its grim
3381𓀴      charm. When
 you are once out

3382𓀴      traces of modern
 England behind

3383𓀴      are conscious everywhere

3384𓀴      prehistoric people. On all

3385𓀴      houses of these

3386𓀴      monoliths

3387𓀴      look at their

3388𓀴      leave your own age behind

3389𓀴      skin-clad, hairy man crawl

3390𓀴      flint-tipped

3391𓀴      that his presence there was

3392𓀴      strange thing is that they

3393𓀴      must always have

3394𓀴      but I could

3395𓀴      race who were

3397𓀴

3400𓀴      practical mind. I can still

3401𓀴      as to whether the sun moved

3402𓀴      the sun. Let me, therefore,

3403𓀴      Henry Baskerville.
3404𓀴
3405𓀴      If you have

3406𓀴      because up

3407𓀴      Then a very surprising

3408𓀴      you in due

3410𓀴
3411𓀴      One of these,

3412𓀴      convict upon

3413𓀴      he has got

3414𓀴      lonely householders of this

3415𓀴      since his flight,

3416𓀴      has been heard

3417𓀴      have held out upon the moor

3419𓀴      one of these

3420𓀴      is nothing to eat unless he

3421𓀴      the moor sheep.

3422𓀴      outlying farmers sleep

3423𓀴
3424𓀴      We are four able-bodied men

3425𓀴      take good

3426𓀴      moments when I have thought

3427𓀴      from any help.

3428𓀴      and the brother, the latter

3429𓀴      helpless in the hands of a

3430𓀴      Hill criminal

3432𓀴      that Perkins

3433𓀴      Stapleton would

3438𓀴      active man

3610𓀴      I have told
 you that the corridor
 is broken by the balcony which
3611𓀴      runs round
 the hall, but that
 it is resumed upon the farther
3612𓀴      side. I waited until he had
 passed out of sight and then I
3613𓀴      followed him.
 When I came round
 the balcony he had reached the
3614𓀴      end of the
 farther corridor,
 and I could see from the glimmer of
3615𓀴      light through
 an open door that
 he had entered one of the rooms.
3616𓀴      Now, all these
 rooms are unfurnished
 and unoccupied so that his
3617𓀴      expedition became
 more mysterious
 than ever. The light shone
3618𓀴      steadily as
 if he were standing
 motionless. I crept down the
3619𓀴      passage as noiselessly as I
 could and peeped round the corner of
3620𓀴      the door.
3621𓀴
3622𓀴      Barrymore was crouching at
 the window with the candle held
3623𓀴      against the
 glass. His profile
 was half turned towards me, and
3624𓀴      his face seemed to be rigid
 with expectation as he stared out
3625𓀴      into the blackness of the
 moor. For some minutes he stood
3626𓀴      watching intently. Then
 he gave a deep groan and with an
3627𓀴      impatient
 gesture he put out
 the light. Instantly I made my way
3628𓀴      back to my room,
 and very shortly
 came the stealthy steps passing
3629𓀴      once more upon their return
 journey. Long afterwards when I had
3630𓀴      fallen into a light sleep I
 heard a key turn somewhere in a lock,
3631𓀴      but I could not tell whence
 the sound came. What it all means I
3632𓀴      cannot guess,
 but there is some
 secret business going on in this
3633𓀴      house of gloom which sooner
 or later we shall get to the bottom
3634𓀴      of. I do not
 trouble you with
 my theories, for you asked me to
3635𓀴      furnish you
 only with facts.
 I have had a long talk with Sir
3636𓀴      Henry this morning, and we
 have made a plan of campaign founded
3637𓀴      upon my observations
 of last
 night. I will not speak about it
3638𓀴      just now,
 but it should make
 my next report interesting reading.
3639𓀴
3640𓀴
3641𓀴
3642𓀴
3643𓀴Chapter 9.
3644𓀴The Light upon the Moor [Second
 Report of Dr. Watson]
3645𓀴
3646𓀴
3647𓀴      Baskerville
 Hall, Oct. 15th.
3648𓀴
3649𓀴      MY DEAR HOLMES,
3650𓀴
3651𓀴      If I was compelled to leave
 you without much news during the
3652𓀴      early days
 of my mission you
 must acknowledge that I am making up
3653𓀴      for lost time,
 and that events
 are now crowding thick and fast
3654𓀴      upon us. In my last report
 I ended upon my top note with
3655𓀴      Barrymore
 at the window, and
 now I have quite a budget already
3656𓀴      which will,
 unless I am much
 mistaken, considerably surprise you.
3657𓀴      Things have
 taken a turn which
 I could not have anticipated. In
3658𓀴      some ways they
 have within the
 last forty-eight hours become much
3659𓀴      clearer and
 in some ways they
 have become more complicated. But I
3660𓀴      will tell you all and
 you shall judge for yourself.
3661𓀴
3662𓀴      Before breakfast
 on the morning
 following my adventure I went
3663𓀴      down the corridor
 and examined
 the room in which Barrymore had
3664𓀴      been on the
 night before. The
 western window through which he had
3665𓀴      stared so intently has, I
 noticed, one peculiarity above all
3666𓀴      other windows
 in the house—it
 commands the nearest outlook on to
3667𓀴      the moor.
 There is an opening
 between two trees which enables one
3668𓀴      from this point of view to
 look right down upon it, while from
3669𓀴      all the other windows it is
 only a distant glimpse which can be
3670𓀴      obtained. It
 follows, therefore,
 that Barrymore, since only this
3671𓀴      window would
 serve the purpose,
 must have been looking out for
3672𓀴      something or
 somebody upon the
 moor. The night was very dark, so
3673𓀴      that I can
 hardly imagine how
 he could have hoped to see anyone.
3674𓀴      It had struck
 me that it was
 possible that some love intrigue was
3675𓀴      on foot. That
 would have accounted
 for his stealthy movements and
3676𓀴      also for the uneasiness
 of his wife. The man is a
3677𓀴      striking-looking
 fellow, very
 well equipped to steal the heart of
3678𓀴      a country
 girl, so that this
 theory seemed to have something to
3679𓀴      support it. That opening of
 the door which I had heard after I
3680𓀴      had returned
 to my room might
 mean that he had gone out to keep
3681𓀴      some clandestine
 appointment.
 So I reasoned with myself in the
3682𓀴      morning, and I tell you the
 direction of my suspicions, however
3683𓀴      much the result may have
 shown that they were unfounded.
3684𓀴
3685𓀴      But whatever
 the true explanation
 of Barrymore’s movements might
3686𓀴      be, I felt that
 the responsibility
 of keeping them to myself
3687𓀴      until I could explain them
 was more than I could bear. I had an
3688𓀴      interview with the baronet
 in his study after breakfast, and I
3689𓀴      told him all
 that I had seen.
 He was less surprised than I had
3690𓀴      expected.
3691𓀴
3692𓀴      “I knew that
 Barrymore walked
 about nights, and I had a mind to
3693𓀴      speak to him
 about it,” said
 he. “Two or three times I have heard
3694𓀴      his steps in
 the passage, coming
 and going, just about the hour
3695𓀴      you name.”
3696𓀴
3697𓀴      “Perhaps then
 he pays a visit
 every night to that particular
3698𓀴      window,” I suggested.
3699𓀴
3700𓀴      “Perhaps he does. If so, we
 should be able to shadow him and see
3701𓀴      what it is
 that he is after.
 I wonder what your friend Holmes
3702𓀴      would do if he were here.”
3703𓀴
3704𓀴      “I believe that he would do
 exactly what you now suggest,” said
3705𓀴      I. “He would
 follow Barrymore
 and see what he did.”
3706𓀴
3707𓀴      “Then we shall
 do it together.”
3708𓀴
3709𓀴      “But surely
 he would hear us.”
3710𓀴
3711𓀴      “The man is
 rather deaf, and
 in any case we must take our chance
3712𓀴      of that. We’ll sit up in
 my room tonight and wait until he
3713𓀴      passes.” Sir Henry rubbed
 his hands with pleasure, and it was
3714𓀴      evident that he
 hailed the adventure
 as a relief to his somewhat
3715𓀴      quiet life upon the moor.
3716𓀴
3717𓀴      The baronet has
 been in communication
 with the architect who
3718𓀴      prepared the plans for Sir
 Charles, and with a contractor from
3719𓀴      London, so
 that we may expect
 great changes to begin here soon.
3720𓀴      There have been decorators
 and furnishers up from Plymouth, and
3721𓀴      it is evident
 that our friend
 has large ideas and means to spare
3722𓀴      no pains or
 expense to restore
 the grandeur of his family. When
3723𓀴      the house is renovated and
 refurnished, all that he will need
3724𓀴      will be a wife
 to make it complete.
 Between ourselves there are
3725𓀴      pretty clear
 signs that this
 will not be wanting if the lady is
3726𓀴      willing, for I have seldom
 seen a man more infatuated with a
3727𓀴      woman than he
 is with our beautiful
 neighbour, Miss Stapleton.
3728𓀴      And yet the
 course of true love
 does not run quite as smoothly as
3729𓀴      one would under
 the circumstances
 expect. Today, for example, its
3730𓀴      surface was
 broken by a very
 unexpected ripple, which has caused
3731𓀴      our friend considerable
 perplexity and annoyance.
3732𓀴
3733𓀴      After the
 conversation which
 I have quoted about Barrymore, Sir
3734𓀴      Henry put on his hat and
 prepared to go out. As a matter of
3735𓀴      course I did the same.
3736𓀴
3737𓀴      “What, are _you_
 coming, Watson?”
 he asked, looking at me in a
3738𓀴      curious way.
3739𓀴
3740𓀴      “That depends
 on whether you
 are going on the moor,” said I.
3741𓀴
3742𓀴      “Yes, I am.”
3743𓀴
3744𓀴      “Well, you know
 what my instructions
 are. I am sorry to intrude,
3745𓀴      but you heard how earnestly
 Holmes insisted that I should not
3746𓀴      leave you,
 and especially that
 you should not go alone upon the
3747𓀴      moor.”
3748𓀴
3749𓀴      Sir Henry put his hand upon
 my shoulder with a pleasant smile.
3750𓀴
3751𓀴      “My dear fellow,”
 said he, “Holmes,
 with all his wisdom, did not
3752𓀴      foresee some
 things which have
 happened since I have been on the
3753𓀴      moor. You understand me? I
 am sure that you are the last man in
3754𓀴      the world who would wish
 to be a spoil-sport. I must go out
3755𓀴      alone.”
3756𓀴
3757𓀴      It put me in a most awkward
 position. I was at a loss what to say
3758𓀴      or what to do, and before I
 had made up my mind he picked up his
3759𓀴      cane and was gone.
3760𓀴
3761𓀴      But when I
 came to think the
 matter over my conscience reproached
3762𓀴      me bitterly
 for having on any
 pretext allowed him to go out of my
3763𓀴      sight. I imagined
 what my feelings
 would be if I had to return to
3764𓀴      you and to
 confess that some
 misfortune had occurred through my
3765𓀴      disregard for
 your instructions.
 I assure you my cheeks flushed
3766𓀴      at the very thought. It
 might not even now be too late to
3767𓀴      overtake him, so I set off
 at once in the direction of Merripit
3768𓀴      House.
3769𓀴
3770𓀴      I hurried along the road at
 the top of my speed without seeing
3771𓀴      anything of
 Sir Henry, until
 I came to the point where the moor
3772𓀴      path branches
 off. There, fearing
 that perhaps I had come in the
3773𓀴      wrong direction after all,
 I mounted a hill from which I could
3774𓀴      command a
 view—the same hill
 which is cut into the dark quarry.
3775𓀴      Thence I saw
 him at once. He
 was on the moor path about a quarter
3776𓀴      of a mile
 off, and a lady was
 by his side who could only be Miss
3777𓀴      Stapleton.
 It was clear that
 there was already an understanding
3778𓀴      between them and that they
 had met by appointment. They were
3779𓀴      walking slowly
 along in deep
 conversation, and I saw her making
3780𓀴      quick little
 movements of her
 hands as if she were very earnest
3781𓀴      in what she
 was saying, while
 he listened intently, and once or
3782𓀴      twice shook
 his head in strong
 dissent. I stood among the rocks
3783𓀴      watching them,
 very much puzzled
 as to what I should do next. To
3784𓀴      follow them
 and break into their
 intimate conversation seemed to
3785𓀴      be an outrage,
 and yet my clear
 duty was never for an instant to
3786𓀴      let him out of my sight.
 To act the spy upon a friend was a
3787𓀴      hateful task.
 Still, I could
 see no better course than to observe
3788𓀴      him from the
 hill, and to clear
 my conscience by confessing to
3789𓀴      him afterwards what I had
 done. It is true that if any sudden
3790𓀴      danger had threatened him I
 was too far away to be of use, and
3791𓀴      yet I am sure that you will
 agree with me that the position was
3792𓀴      very difficult,
 and that there
 was nothing more which I could do.
3793𓀴
3794𓀴      Our friend, Sir Henry, and
 the lady had halted on the path and
3795𓀴      were standing
 deeply absorbed

3796𓀴      suddenly aware that I was

3797𓀴      interview. A

3798𓀴      another glance

 it was carried on a stick by a man
3799𓀴      who was moving

3800𓀴      butterfly-net. He was very

3801𓀴      and he appeared

3802𓀴      Sir Henry

 Stapleton to his side. His arm was
3803𓀴      round her, but it seemed to

3804𓀴      him with her face averted.
 He stooped his head to hers, and she
3805𓀴      raised one hand

 Next moment I saw them spring
3806𓀴      apart and turn
 hurriedly round.
 Stapleton was the cause of the
3807𓀴      interruption.
 He was running
 wildly towards them, his absurd net
3808𓀴      dangling behind
 him. He gesticulated
 and almost danced with
3809𓀴      excitement in front of the

3810𓀴      not imagine, but it seemed

3811𓀴      Henry, who offered
 explanations,
 which became more angry as the
3812𓀴      other refused
 to accept them.
 The lady stood by in haughty
3813𓀴      silence. Finally

3814𓀴      peremptory

 who, after an irresolute glance at
3815𓀴      Sir Henry, walked off

3816𓀴      naturalist’s angry gestures
 showed that the lady was included in
3817𓀴      his displeasure.
 The baronet

3818𓀴      them, and then
 he walked slowly

3819𓀴      his head hanging, the

3820𓀴
3821𓀴      What all this meant I could

3822𓀴      to have witnessed

3823𓀴      knowledge.
 I ran down the hill
 therefore and met the baronet at
3824𓀴      the bottom.
 His face was flushed
 with anger and his brows were
3825𓀴      wrinkled, like one who

3826𓀴
3827𓀴      “Halloa, Watson! Where have
 you dropped from?” said he. “You
3828𓀴      don’t mean to say that you

3829𓀴
3830𓀴      I explained

 how I had found it impossible to
3831𓀴      remain behind,
 how I had followed

3832𓀴      all that had

3833𓀴      my frankness disarmed his

3834𓀴      rather rueful laugh.
3835𓀴
3836𓀴      “You would have thought the

3837𓀴      place for a

 said he, “but, by thunder, the
3838𓀴      whole countryside seems

3839𓀴      wooing—and
