 at that! Where had you engaged a
3840𓀴      seat?”
3841𓀴
3842𓀴      “I was on that hill.”
3843𓀴
3844𓀴      “Quite in the back row, eh?
 But her brother was well up to the
3845𓀴      front. Did you

3846𓀴
3847𓀴      “Yes, I did.”
3848𓀴
3849𓀴      “Did he ever strike you as

3850𓀴
3851𓀴      “I can’t say
 that he ever did.”
3852𓀴
3853𓀴      “I dare say

3854𓀴      but you can take it from me

3855𓀴      straitjacket.

3856𓀴      near me for

3857𓀴      anything that would prevent

3858𓀴      woman that I loved?”
3859𓀴
3860𓀴      “I should say not.”
3861𓀴
3862𓀴      “He can’t object

3863𓀴      that he has

3864𓀴      man or woman

3865𓀴      much as let me touch

3866𓀴
3867𓀴      “Did he say so?”
3868𓀴
3869𓀴      “That, and

3870𓀴      these few

3871𓀴      for me, and

3872𓀴      I’ll swear. There’s a light

3874𓀴      today for

3875𓀴      words with

3876𓀴      it was not

3877𓀴      let me talk about it either

3878𓀴      kept coming back to it that

3879𓀴      she would never

3880𓀴      since I had seen her I was

3881𓀴      she really wanted me to go,

3883𓀴      marry her,

3884𓀴      hers, running

3885𓀴      white with rage, and those

3886𓀴      fury. What was I doing with

3887𓀴      attentions which

3888𓀴      because I was

3889𓀴      been her brother

3890𓀴      it was I told

3891𓀴      as I was not

3892𓀴      me by becoming

3893𓀴      so then I

3894𓀴      hotly than

3895𓀴      by. So it

3896𓀴      am I as badly puzzled a man

3897𓀴      what it all

3898𓀴      hope to pay.”
3899𓀴
3900𓀴      I tried one
 or two explanations,
 but, indeed, I was completely
3901𓀴      puzzled myself.
 Our friend’s

3902𓀴      character,

3903𓀴      nothing against

3904𓀴      family. That

3905𓀴      any reference to the lady’s

3906𓀴      accept the

3907𓀴      our conjectures were set

3908𓀴      himself that

 He had come to offer apologies for
3909𓀴      his rudeness

3910𓀴      with Sir Henry in his study

3911𓀴      that the breach is quite
 healed, and that we are to dine at
3912𓀴      Merripit House next
 Friday as a sign of it.
3913𓀴
3914𓀴      “I don’t say
 now that he isn’t
 a crazy man,” said Sir Henry; “I
3915𓀴      can’t forget
 the look in his
 eyes when he ran at me this morning,
3916𓀴      but I must

 could make a more handsome apology
3917𓀴      than he has done.”
3918𓀴
3919𓀴      “Did he give

3920𓀴
3921𓀴      “His sister
 is everything in
 his life, he says. That is natural
3922𓀴      enough, and I am glad that
 he should understand her value. They
3923𓀴      have always been together,
 and according to his account he has
3924𓀴      been a very lonely man with

3925𓀴      thought of losing her was

3926𓀴      understood, he said, that
 I was becoming attached to her, but
3927𓀴      when he saw

3928𓀴      might be taken

3929𓀴      time he was not responsible

3930𓀴      sorry for all

 and he recognized how foolish and
3931𓀴      how selfish it was that he

3932𓀴      beautiful woman

3933𓀴      she had to leave him he had

3934𓀴      myself than to anyone else.
 But in any case it was a blow to him
3935𓀴      and it would take him some

3936𓀴      to meet it.
 He would withdraw

3937𓀴      would promise

3938𓀴      content with

3939𓀴      without claiming her love.
 This I promised, and so the matter
3940𓀴      rests.”
3941𓀴
3942𓀴      So there is
 one of our small
 mysteries cleared up. It is
3943𓀴      something to

3944𓀴      are floundering.
 We know now

3945𓀴      upon his sister’s

3946𓀴      one as Sir Henry. And now

3947𓀴      have extricated

3948𓀴      in the night,

3949𓀴      secret journey

 to the western lattice window.
3950𓀴      Congratulate
 me, my dear Holmes,
 and tell me that I have not
3951𓀴      disappointed you as an
 agent—that you do not regret the
3952𓀴      confidence which you showed

3953𓀴      these things

3954𓀴
3955𓀴      I have said “by one night’s

3956𓀴      nights’ work,

3957𓀴      with Sir Henry in his rooms

3958𓀴      morning, but
 no sound of any

3959𓀴      clock upon

3960𓀴      by each of

3961𓀴      not discouraged,
 and we determined

3962𓀴      we lowered the lamp and sat

3963𓀴      least sound.
 It was incredible

3964𓀴      and yet we

4350𓀴      baronet, but in the instant

4351𓀴      his arm the
 man was gone. There
 was the sharp pinnacle of granite
4352𓀴      still cutting
 the lower edge
 of the moon, but its peak bore no
4353𓀴      trace of that silent
 and motionless figure.
4354𓀴
4355𓀴      I wished to
 go in that direction
 and to search the tor, but it
4356𓀴      was some distance away. The
 baronet’s nerves were still quivering
4357𓀴      from that
 cry, which recalled
 the dark story of his family, and
4358𓀴      he was not in
 the mood for fresh
 adventures. He had not seen this
4359𓀴      lonely man upon the tor and
 could not feel the thrill which his
4360𓀴      strange presence
 and his commanding
 attitude had given to me. “A
4361𓀴      warder, no doubt,” said he.
 “The moor has been thick with them
4362𓀴      since this fellow escaped.”
 Well, perhaps his explanation may be
4363𓀴      the right one, but I should
 like to have some further proof of
4364𓀴      it. Today we
 mean to communicate
 to the Princetown people where
4365𓀴      they should look
 for their missing
 man, but it is hard lines that
4366𓀴      we have not
 actually had the
 triumph of bringing him back as our
4367𓀴      own prisoner.
 Such are the adventures
 of last night, and you must
4368𓀴      acknowledge,
 my dear Holmes,
 that I have done you very well in
4369𓀴      the matter
 of a report. Much
 of what I tell you is no doubt quite
4370𓀴      irrelevant,
 but still I feel
 that it is best that I should let
4371𓀴      you have all
 the facts and leave
 you to select for yourself those
4372𓀴      which will
 be of most service
 to you in helping you to your
4373𓀴      conclusions.
 We are certainly
 making some progress. So far as the
4374𓀴      Barrymores go we have found
 the motive of their actions, and that
4375𓀴      has cleared
 up the situation
 very much. But the moor with its
4376𓀴      mysteries and
 its strange inhabitants
 remains as inscrutable as
4377𓀴      ever. Perhaps in my next I
 may be able to throw some light upon
4378𓀴      this also.
 Best of all would
 it be if you could come down to us.
4379𓀴      In any case
 you will hear from
 me again in the course of the next
4380𓀴      few days.
4381𓀴
4382𓀴
4383𓀴
4384𓀴
4385𓀴Chapter 10.
4386𓀴Extract from the
 Diary of Dr. Watson
4387𓀴
4388𓀴
4389𓀴      So far I have been able to
 quote from the reports which I have
4390𓀴      forwarded
 during these early
 days to Sherlock Holmes. Now,
4391𓀴      however, I have arrived at
 a point in my narrative where I am
4392𓀴      compelled to abandon this
 method and to trust once more to my
4393𓀴      recollections, aided by the
 diary which I kept at the time. A few
4394𓀴      extracts from
 the latter will
 carry me on to those scenes which
4395𓀴      are indelibly
 fixed in every
 detail upon my memory. I proceed,
4396𓀴      then, from
 the morning which
 followed our abortive chase of the
4397𓀴      convict and
 our other strange
 experiences upon the moor.
4398𓀴
4399𓀴      _October_
 16_th_.—A dull and
 foggy day with a drizzle of rain.
4400𓀴      The house is banked in with
 rolling clouds, which rise now and
4401𓀴      then to show
 the dreary curves
 of the moor, with thin, silver
4402𓀴      veins upon the sides of the
 hills, and the distant boulders
4403𓀴      gleaming where
 the light strikes
 upon their wet faces. It is
4404𓀴      melancholy outside and in.
 The baronet is in a black reaction
4405𓀴      after the
 excitements of the
 night. I am conscious myself of a
4406𓀴      weight at my
 heart and a feeling
 of impending danger—ever present
4407𓀴      danger, which
 is the more terrible
 because I am unable to define
4408𓀴      it.
4409𓀴
4410𓀴      And have I not cause for
 such a feeling? Consider the long
4411𓀴      sequence of incidents which
 have all pointed to some sinister
4412𓀴      influence which is at work
 around us. There is the death of the
4413𓀴      last occupant of the Hall,
 fulfilling so exactly the conditions
4414𓀴      of the family legend, and
 there are the repeated reports from
4415𓀴      peasants of the appearance
 of a strange creature upon the moor.
4416𓀴      Twice I have
 with my own ears
 heard the sound which resembled the
4417𓀴      distant baying
 of a hound. It
 is incredible, impossible, that it
4418𓀴      should really
 be outside the
 ordinary laws of nature. A spectral
4419𓀴      hound which leaves material
 footmarks and fills the air with its
4420𓀴      howling is surely not to be
 thought of. Stapleton may fall in
4421𓀴      with such
 a superstition, and
 Mortimer also, but if I have one
4422𓀴      quality upon
 earth it is common
 sense, and nothing will persuade
4423𓀴      me to believe
 in such a thing.
 To do so would be to descend to
4424𓀴      the level of
 these poor peasants,
 who are not content with a mere
4425𓀴      fiend dog but
 must needs describe
 him with hell-fire shooting
4426𓀴      from his mouth
 and eyes. Holmes
 would not listen to such fancies,
4427𓀴      and I am his
 agent. But facts
 are facts, and I have twice heard
4428𓀴      this crying upon the moor.
 Suppose that there were really some
4429𓀴      huge hound loose upon it;
 that would go far to explain
4430𓀴      everything. But where could
 such a hound lie concealed, where did
4431𓀴      it get its food, where did
 it come from, how was it that no one
4432𓀴      saw it by day?
 It must be confessed
 that the natural explanation
4433𓀴      offers almost
 as many difficulties
 as the other. And always,
4434𓀴      apart from the hound, there
 is the fact of the human agency in
4435𓀴      London, the man in the cab,
 and the letter which warned Sir Henry
4436𓀴      against the
 moor. This at least
 was real, but it might have been
4437𓀴      the work of
 a protecting friend
 as easily as of an enemy. Where
4438𓀴      is that friend
 or enemy now?
 Has he remained in London, or has he
4439𓀴      followed us
 down here? Could
 he—could he be the stranger whom I
4440𓀴      saw upon the tor?
4441𓀴
4442𓀴      It is true that I have had
 only the one glance at him, and yet
4443𓀴      there are some
 things to which
 I am ready to swear. He is no one
4444𓀴      whom I have seen down here,
 and I have now met all the
4445𓀴      neighbours.
 The figure was far
 taller than that of Stapleton, far
4446𓀴      thinner than
 that of Frankland.
 Barrymore it might possibly have
4447𓀴      been, but we had left him
 behind us, and I am certain that he
4448𓀴      could not have followed us.
 A stranger then is still dogging us,
4449𓀴      just as a
 stranger dogged us
 in London. We have never shaken him
4450𓀴      off. If I
 could lay my hands
 upon that man, then at last we might
4451𓀴      find ourselves
 at the end of
 all our difficulties. To this one
4452𓀴      purpose I must now
 devote all my energies.
4453𓀴
4454𓀴      My first impulse
 was to tell
 Sir Henry all my plans. My second
4455𓀴      and wisest one is to play
 my own game and speak as little as
4456𓀴      possible to
 anyone. He is silent
 and distrait. His nerves have
4457𓀴      been strangely
 shaken by that
 sound upon the moor. I will say
4458𓀴      nothing to add
 to his anxieties,
 but I will take my own steps to
4459𓀴      attain my own end.
4460𓀴
4461𓀴      We had a small scene this
 morning after breakfast. Barrymore
4462𓀴      asked leave
 to speak with Sir
 Henry, and they were closeted in
4463𓀴      his study some little time.
 Sitting in the billiard-room I more
4464𓀴      than once
 heard the sound of
 voices raised, and I had a pretty
4465𓀴      good idea
 what the point was
 which was under discussion. After a
4466𓀴      time the baronet opened his
 door and called for me. “Barrymore
4467𓀴      considers that
 he has a grievance,”
 he said. “He thinks that it
4468𓀴      was unfair
 on our part to hunt
 his brother-in-law down when he,
4469𓀴      of his own free will,
 had told us the secret.”
4470𓀴
4471𓀴      The butler
 was standing very
 pale but very collected before us.
4472𓀴
4473𓀴      “I may have
 spoken too warmly,
 sir,” said he, “and if I have, I
4474𓀴      am sure that
 I beg your pardon.
 At the same time, I was very much
4475𓀴      surprised when I heard you

4476𓀴      and learned

4477𓀴      enough to fight

4478𓀴
4479𓀴      “If you had told us of your

4480𓀴      different thing,”
 said the baronet,
 “you only told us, or rather
4481𓀴      your wife

4482𓀴      not help yourself.”
4483𓀴
4484𓀴      “I didn’t think you would

4485𓀴      Henry—indeed I didn’t.”
4486𓀴
4487𓀴      “The man is

 There are lonely houses scattered
4488𓀴      over the moor,

 who would stick at nothing. You
4489𓀴      only want to get a glimpse

4490𓀴      Stapleton’s

4491𓀴      it. There’s

4492𓀴
4493𓀴      “He’ll break into no house,

4494𓀴      that. But he

4495𓀴      assure you, Sir Henry, that

4496𓀴      arrangements will have been

4497𓀴      South America.
 For God’s sake,

4498𓀴      police know

4499𓀴      chase there, and he can lie

4500𓀴      him. You can’t tell on him

4501𓀴      trouble. I beg you, sir,

4502𓀴
4503𓀴      “What do you say, Watson?”
4504𓀴
4505𓀴      I shrugged
 my shoulders. “If
 he were safely out of the country it
4506𓀴      would relieve the
 tax-payer of a burden.”
4507𓀴
4508𓀴      “But how about the chance

4509𓀴      goes?”
4510𓀴
4511𓀴      “He would
 not do anything so
 mad, sir. We have provided him with
4512𓀴      all that he
 can want. To commit
 a crime would be to show where he
4513𓀴      was hiding.”
4514𓀴
4515𓀴      “That is true,” said
 Sir Henry. “Well, Barrymore—”
4516𓀴
4517𓀴      “God bless
 you, sir, and thank
 you from my heart! It would have
4518𓀴      killed my poor wife

4519𓀴
4520𓀴      “I guess we are
 aiding and abetting

4521𓀴      what we have
 heard I don’t feel
 as if I could give the man up, so
4522𓀴      there is an end of it. All

4523𓀴
4524𓀴      With a few broken words of

4525𓀴      hesitated

4526𓀴
4527𓀴      “You’ve been so kind to us,
 sir, that I should like to do the
4528𓀴      best I can

4529𓀴      perhaps I should have said

4530𓀴      inquest that

4531𓀴      yet to mortal man. It’s

4532𓀴
4533𓀴      The baronet and I were both

4534𓀴      died?”
4535𓀴
4536𓀴      “No, sir,
 I don’t know that.”
4537𓀴
4538𓀴      “What then?”
4539𓀴
4540𓀴      “I know why he was at the

4541𓀴      woman.”
4542𓀴
4543𓀴      “To meet a woman! He?”
4544𓀴
4545𓀴      “Yes, sir.”
4546𓀴
4547𓀴      “And the woman’s name?”
4548𓀴
4549𓀴      “I can’t give you the name,

4550𓀴      Her initials were L. L.”
4551𓀴
4552𓀴      “How do you know

4553𓀴
4554𓀴      “Well, Sir

4555𓀴      usually a

4556𓀴      known for

4557𓀴      glad to turn

4558𓀴      only this one letter, so I

4559𓀴      from Coombe Tracey, and it

4560𓀴
4561𓀴      “Well?”
4562𓀴

4564𓀴      done had it not been for my

4565𓀴      cleaning out

4566𓀴      his death—and she found the

4567𓀴      of the grate.
 The greater part

4568𓀴      one little slip, the end

4569𓀴      writing could

4570𓀴      ground. It seemed to us to

4571𓀴      letter and

4572𓀴      this letter, and be at the

4573𓀴      signed the initials L. L.”
4574𓀴
4575𓀴      “Have you got that slip?”
4576𓀴
4577𓀴      “No, sir, it crumbled

4578𓀴
4579𓀴      “Had Sir Charles
 received any

4580𓀴
4581𓀴      “Well, sir,

4582𓀴      not have noticed this one,

4583𓀴
4584𓀴      “And you have
 no idea who L. L. is?”
4585𓀴
4586𓀴      “No, sir. No

 But I expect if we could lay our
4587𓀴      hands upon

4588𓀴      death.”
4589𓀴
4590𓀴      “I cannot understand,

4591𓀴      important information.”
4592𓀴
4593𓀴      “Well, sir,
 it was immediately

4594𓀴      us. And then again, sir, we

4595𓀴      Charles, as

4596𓀴      us. To rake

4597𓀴      to go carefully

4598𓀴      us—”
4599𓀴
4600𓀴      “You thought it might
 injure his reputation?”
4601𓀴
4602𓀴      “Well, sir,
 I thought no good
 could come of it. But now you have
4603𓀴      been kind to us, and I feel
 as if it would be treating you
4604𓀴      unfairly not to tell you

4605𓀴
4606𓀴      “Very good, Barrymore; you
 can go.” When the butler had left us
4607𓀴      Sir Henry turned

 Watson, what do you think of this
4608𓀴      new light?”
4609𓀴
4610𓀴      “It seems to
 leave the darkness

4611𓀴
4612𓀴      “So I think. But if we can
 only trace L. L. it should clear up
4613𓀴      the whole business. We have

4614𓀴      is someone
 who has the facts

4615𓀴      think we should do?”
4616𓀴
4617𓀴      “Let Holmes
 know all about it

4618𓀴      for which he
 has been seeking.
 I am much mistaken if it does not
4619𓀴      bring him down.”
4620𓀴
4621𓀴      I went at
 once to my room and
 drew up my report of the morning’s
4622𓀴      conversation for Holmes. It
 was evident to me that he had been
4623𓀴      very busy of late, for the
 notes which I had from Baker Street
4624𓀴      were few and short, with no

4625𓀴      had supplied and hardly any

4626𓀴      blackmailing
 case is absorbing

4627𓀴      new factor
 must surely arrest
 his attention and renew his
4628𓀴      interest. I wish
 that he were here.
4629𓀴
4630𓀴      _October_ 17_th_.—All
 day today
 the rain poured down, rustling on
4631𓀴      the ivy and
 dripping from the
 eaves. I thought of the convict out
4632𓀴      upon the bleak,
 cold, shelterless

4633𓀴      crimes, he has
 suffered something

4634𓀴      thought of
 that other one—the

4635𓀴      the moon.
 Was he also out in
 that deluged—the unseen watcher, the
4636𓀴      man of darkness?
 In the evening

4637𓀴      walked far upon the sodden
 moor, full of dark imaginings, the
4638𓀴      rain beating

 the wind whistling about my ears.
4639𓀴      God help those
 who wander into
 the great mire now, for even the
4640𓀴      firm uplands are becoming a

4641𓀴      which I had
 seen the solitary
 watcher, and from its craggy summit
4642𓀴      I looked out myself across

4643𓀴      drifted across their russet
 face, and the heavy, slate-coloured
4644𓀴      clouds hung low
 over the landscape,
 trailing in grey wreaths down
4645𓀴      the sides of the fantastic

4646𓀴      left, half
 hidden by the mist,

4647𓀴      Hall rose above

 were the only signs of human life
4648𓀴      which I could

4649𓀴      thickly upon
 the slopes of the

4650𓀴      of that lonely

 seen on the same spot two nights
4651𓀴      before.
4652𓀴
4653𓀴      As I walked
 back I was overtaken

4654𓀴      dog-cart over

4655𓀴      farmhouse of Foulmire. He
 has been very attentive to us, and
4656𓀴      hardly a day
 has passed that

4657𓀴      how we were getting on. He

4658𓀴      dog-cart, and he gave me

4659𓀴      troubled over

4660𓀴      wandered on to the moor and

4661𓀴      consolation as I might, but
 I thought of the pony on the Grimpen
4662𓀴      Mire, and

 he will see his little dog again.
4663𓀴
4664𓀴      “By the way,
 Mortimer,” said
 I as we jolted along the rough road,
4665𓀴      “I suppose

 living within driving distance of
4666𓀴      this whom you do not know?”
4667𓀴
4668𓀴      “Hardly any, I think.”
4669𓀴
4670𓀴      “Can you, then, tell me the
 name of any woman whose initials are
4671𓀴      L. L.?”
4672𓀴
4673𓀴      He thought

4674𓀴
4675𓀴      “No,” said he. “There are a
 few gipsies and labouring folk for
4676𓀴      whom I can’t

4677𓀴      one whose

 Wait a bit though,” he added after
4678𓀴      a pause. “There

4679𓀴      lives in Coombe Tracey.”
4680𓀴
4681𓀴      “Who is she?” I asked.
4682𓀴
4683𓀴      “She is Frankland’s

4684𓀴
4685𓀴      “What! Old Frankland
 the crank?”
4686𓀴
4687𓀴      “Exactly. She
 married an artist
 named Lyons, who came sketching
4688𓀴      on the moor.
 He proved to be

4689𓀴      fault from what I hear may

4690𓀴      Her father refused to have
 anything to do with her because she
4691𓀴      had married

4692𓀴      reasons as

4693𓀴      girl has had

4694𓀴
4695𓀴      “How does she live?”
4696𓀴
4697𓀴      “I fancy old
 Frankland allows
 her a pittance, but it cannot be
4698𓀴      more, for
 his own affairs are

4699𓀴      may have deserved one could

4700𓀴      bad. Her story got about,

4701𓀴      something to enable her to

4702𓀴      for one, and

4703𓀴      was to set her up

4704𓀴
4705𓀴      He wanted

4706𓀴      satisfy his

4707𓀴      no reason

4710𓀴      have been made

4711𓀴      mysteries. I am

4712𓀴      for when Mortimer

4713𓀴      I asked him

4714𓀴      so heard nothing

4715𓀴      not lived for years with

4716𓀴
4717𓀴      I have only
 one other incident
 to record upon this tempestuous
4718𓀴      and melancholy
 day. This was
 my conversation with Barrymore just
4719𓀴      now, which
 gives me one more
 strong card which I can play in due
4720𓀴      time.
4721𓀴
4722𓀴      Mortimer had
 stayed to dinner,
 and he and the baronet played
4723𓀴      écarté afterwards.
 The butler
 brought me my coffee into the
4724𓀴      library, and
 I took the chance
 to ask him a few questions.
4725𓀴
4726𓀴      “Well,” said
 I, “has this precious
 relation of yours departed, or
4727𓀴      is he still
 lurking out yonder?”
4728𓀴
4729𓀴      “I don’t know,
 sir. I hope to
 heaven that he has gone, for he has
4730𓀴      brought nothing but trouble
 here! I’ve not heard of him since I
4731𓀴      left out food for him last,
 and that was three days ago.”
4732𓀴
4733𓀴      “Did you see him then?”
4734𓀴
4735𓀴      “No, sir, but the food was
 gone when next I went that way.”
4736𓀴
4737𓀴      “Then he was
 certainly there?”
4738𓀴
4739𓀴      “So you would
 think, sir, unless
 it was the other man who took
4740𓀴      it.”
4741𓀴
4742𓀴      I sat with my coffee-cup
 halfway to my lips and stared at
4743𓀴      Barrymore.
4744𓀴
4745𓀴      “You know that there
 is another man then?”
4746𓀴
4747𓀴      “Yes, sir; there is
 another man upon the moor.”
4748𓀴
4749𓀴      “Have you seen him?”
4750𓀴
4751𓀴      “No, sir.”
4752𓀴
4753𓀴      “How do you
 know of him then?”
4754𓀴
4755𓀴      “Selden told
 me of him, sir,
 a week ago or more. He’s in hiding,
4756𓀴      too, but he’s not a convict
 as far as I can make out. I don’t
4757𓀴      like it, Dr. Watson—I tell
 you straight, sir, that I don’t like
4758𓀴      it.” He spoke with a
 sudden passion of earnestness.
4759𓀴
4760𓀴      “Now, listen
 to me, Barrymore!
 I have no interest in this matter
4761𓀴      but that of your master. I
 have come here with no object except
4762𓀴      to help him.
 Tell me, frankly,
 what it is that you don’t like.”
4763𓀴
4764𓀴      Barrymore hesitated
 for a moment,
 as if he regretted his outburst
4765𓀴      or found it difficult to
 express his own feelings in words.
4766𓀴
4767𓀴      “It’s all these goings-on,
 sir,” he cried at last, waving his
4768𓀴      hand towards
 the rain-lashed
 window which faced the moor.
4769𓀴      “There’s foul
 play somewhere,
 and there’s black villainy brewing,
4770𓀴      to that I’ll
 swear! Very glad
 I should be, sir, to see Sir Henry
4771𓀴      on his way back
 to London again!”
4772𓀴
4773𓀴      “But what is
 it that alarms you?”
4774𓀴
4775𓀴      “Look at Sir
 Charles’s death!
 That was bad enough, for all that
4776𓀴      the coroner said. Look at
 the noises on the moor at night.
4777𓀴      There’s not
 a man would cross
 it after sundown if he was paid for
4778𓀴      it. Look at this stranger
 hiding out yonder, and watching and
4779𓀴      waiting! What’s he waiting
 for? What does it mean? It means no
4780𓀴      good to anyone
 of the name of
 Baskerville, and very glad I shall
4781𓀴      be to be quit
 of it all on the
 day that Sir Henry’s new servants
4782𓀴      are ready to
 take over the Hall.”
4783𓀴
4784𓀴      “But about this stranger,”
 said I. “Can you tell me anything
4785𓀴      about him?
 What did Selden say?
 Did he find out where he hid, or
4786𓀴      what he was doing?”
4787𓀴
4788𓀴      “He saw him
 once or twice, but
 he is a deep one and gives nothing
4789𓀴      away. At first he thought
 that he was the police, but soon he
4790𓀴      found that he had some lay
 of his own. A kind of gentleman he
4791𓀴      was, as far
 as he could see,
 but what he was doing he could not
4792𓀴      make out.”
4793𓀴
4794𓀴      “And where did
 he say that he lived?”
4795𓀴
4796𓀴      “Among the
 old houses on the
 hillside—the stone huts where the
4797𓀴      old folk used to live.”
4798𓀴
4799𓀴      “But how about his food?”
4800𓀴
4801𓀴      “Selden found out that he
 has got a lad who works for him and
4802𓀴      brings all he needs. I dare
 say he goes to Coombe Tracey for what
4803𓀴      he wants.”
4804𓀴
4805𓀴      “Very good, Barrymore. We
 may talk further of this some other
4806𓀴      time.” When the butler had
 gone I walked over to the black
4807𓀴      window, and
 I looked through
 a blurred pane at the driving clouds
4808𓀴      and at the
 tossing outline of
 the wind-swept trees. It is a wild
4809𓀴      night indoors,
 and what must
 it be in a stone hut upon the moor.
4810𓀴      What passion of hatred can
 it be which leads a man to lurk in
4811𓀴      such a place
 at such a time!
 And what deep and earnest purpose
4812𓀴      can he have which calls for
 such a trial! There, in that hut upon
4813𓀴      the moor, seems
 to lie the very
 centre of that problem which has
4814𓀴      vexed me so sorely. I swear
 that another day shall not have
4815𓀴      passed before
 I have done all
 that man can do to reach the heart
4816𓀴      of the mystery.
4817𓀴
4818𓀴
4819𓀴
4820𓀴
4821𓀴Chapter 11.
4822𓀴The Man on the Tor
4823𓀴
4824𓀴
4825𓀴      The extract from my private
 diary which forms the last chapter
4826𓀴      has brought my narrative up
 to the eighteenth of October, a time
4827𓀴      when these strange events
 began to move swiftly towards their
4828𓀴      terrible conclusion. The
 incidents of the next few days are
4829𓀴      indelibly graven upon my
 recollection, and I can tell them
4830𓀴      without reference
 to the notes
 made at the time. I start them
4831𓀴      from the day
 which succeeded
 that upon which I had established
4832𓀴      two facts of
 great importance,
 the one that Mrs. Laura Lyons of
4833𓀴      Coombe Tracey
 had written to
 Sir Charles Baskerville and made an
4834𓀴      appointment with him at the
 very place and hour that he met his
4835𓀴      death, the other that the
 lurking man upon the moor was to be
4836𓀴      found among the stone huts
 upon the hillside. With these two
4837𓀴      facts in my
 possession I felt
 that either my intelligence or my
4838𓀴      courage must
 be deficient if
 I could not throw some further light
4839𓀴      upon these dark places.
4840𓀴
4841𓀴      I had no opportunity
 to tell
 the baronet what I had learned about
4842𓀴      Mrs. Lyons upon the evening
 before, for Dr. Mortimer remained
4843𓀴      with him at
 cards until it was
 very late. At breakfast, however,
4844𓀴      I informed him
 about my discovery
 and asked him whether he would
4845𓀴      care to accompany
 me to Coombe
 Tracey. At first he was very eager
4846𓀴      to come, but
 on second thoughts
 it seemed to both of us that if I
4847𓀴      went alone
 the results might
 be better. The more formal we made
4848𓀴      the visit the
 less information
 we might obtain. I left Sir Henry
4849𓀴      behind, therefore,
 not without
 some prickings of conscience, and
4850𓀴      drove off
 upon my new quest.
4851𓀴
4852𓀴      When I reached
 Coombe Tracey
 I told Perkins to put up the horses,
4853𓀴      and I made
 inquiries for the
 lady whom I had come to interrogate.
4854𓀴      I had no difficulty
 in finding
 her rooms, which were central and
4855𓀴      well appointed.
 A maid showed
 me in without ceremony, and as I
4856𓀴      entered the sitting-room
 a lady, who was sitting before a
4857𓀴      Remington
 typewriter, sprang
 up with a pleasant smile of welcome.
4858𓀴      Her face fell,
 however, when
 she saw that I was a stranger, and
4859𓀴      she sat down again and
 asked me the object of my visit.
4860𓀴
4861𓀴      The first impression left
 by Mrs. Lyons was one of extreme
4862𓀴      beauty. Her
 eyes and hair were
 of the same rich hazel colour, and
4863𓀴      her cheeks,
 though considerably
 freckled, were flushed with the
4864𓀴      exquisite bloom
 of the brunette,
 the dainty pink which lurks at
4865𓀴      the heart of
 the sulphur rose.
 Admiration was, I repeat, the
4866𓀴      first impression. But the
 second was criticism. There was
4867𓀴      something subtly wrong with
 the face, some coarseness of
4868𓀴      expression, some
 hardness, perhaps,
 of eye, some looseness of lip
4869𓀴      which marred its perfect
 beauty. But these, of course, are
4870𓀴      afterthoughts.
 At the moment
 I was simply conscious that I was in
4871𓀴      the presence
 of a very handsome
 woman, and that she was asking me
4872𓀴      the reasons for my visit. I
 had not quite understood until that
4873𓀴      instant how delicate
 my mission was.
4874𓀴
4875𓀴      “I have the pleasure,” said
 I, “of knowing your father.”
4876𓀴
4877𓀴      It was a clumsy
 introduction,
 and the lady made me feel it.
4878𓀴      “There is nothing in common
 between my father and me,” she said.
4879𓀴      “I owe him nothing, and his
 friends are not mine. If it were not
4880𓀴      for the late Sir
 Charles Baskerville
 and some other kind hearts I
4881𓀴      might have starved for
 all that my father cared.”
4882𓀴
4883𓀴      “It was about the late Sir
 Charles Baskerville that I have come
4884𓀴      here to see you.”
4885𓀴
4886𓀴      The freckles started
 out on the lady’s face.
4887𓀴
4888𓀴      “What can I tell you about
 him?” she asked, and her fingers
4889𓀴      played nervously over
 the stops of her typewriter.
4890𓀴
4891𓀴      “You knew
 him, did you not?”
4892𓀴
4893𓀴      “I have already said that I
 owe a great deal to his kindness. If
4894𓀴      I am able to support myself
 it is largely due to the interest
4895𓀴      which he took in
 my unhappy situation.”
4896𓀴
4897𓀴      “Did you correspond
 with him?”
4898𓀴
4899𓀴      The lady looked
 quickly up with
 an angry gleam in her hazel eyes.
4900𓀴
4901𓀴      “What is the
 object of these
 questions?” she asked sharply.
4902𓀴
4903𓀴      “The object is to avoid a
 public scandal. It is better that I
4904𓀴      should ask
 them here than that
 the matter should pass outside our
4905𓀴      control.”
4906𓀴
4907𓀴      She was silent and her face
 was still very pale. At last she
4908𓀴      looked up with
 something reckless
 and defiant in her manner.
4909𓀴
4910𓀴      “Well, I’ll answer,” she
 said. “What are your questions?”
4911𓀴
4912𓀴      “Did you correspond
 with Sir Charles?”
4913𓀴
4914𓀴      “I certainly wrote to him
 once or twice to acknowledge his
4915𓀴      delicacy and
 his generosity.”
4916𓀴
4917𓀴      “Have you the dates
 of those letters?”
4918𓀴
4919𓀴      “No.”
4920𓀴
4921𓀴      “Have you ever met him?”
4922𓀴
4923𓀴      “Yes, once
 or twice, when he
 came into Coombe Tracey. He was a
4924𓀴      very retiring man, and he
 preferred to do good by stealth.”
4925𓀴
4926𓀴      “But if you
 saw him so seldom
 and wrote so seldom, how did he
4927𓀴      know enough
 about your affairs
 to be able to help you, as you say
4928𓀴      that he has done?”
4929𓀴
4930𓀴      She met my difficulty
 with the utmost readiness.
4931𓀴
4932𓀴      “There were
 several gentlemen
 who knew my sad history and united
4933𓀴      to help me. One
 was Mr. Stapleton,
 a neighbour and intimate
4934𓀴      friend of Sir Charles’s. He
 was exceedingly kind, and it was
4935𓀴      through him
 that Sir Charles
 learned about my affairs.”
4936𓀴
4937𓀴      I knew already
 that Sir Charles
 Baskerville had made Stapleton
4938𓀴      his almoner upon
 several occasions,
 so the lady’s statement bore
4939𓀴      the impress
 of truth upon it.
4940𓀴
4941𓀴      “Did you ever write to Sir
 Charles asking him to meet you?” I
4942𓀴      continued.
4943𓀴
4944𓀴      Mrs. Lyons
 flushed with anger
 again. “Really, sir, this is a very
4945𓀴      extraordinary question.”
4946𓀴
4947𓀴      “I am sorry, madam,
 but I must repeat it.”
4948𓀴
4949𓀴      “Then I answer,
 certainly not.”
4950𓀴
4951𓀴      “Not on the very day
 of Sir Charles’s death?”
4952𓀴
4953𓀴      The flush had
 faded in an instant,
 and a deathly face was before
4954𓀴      me. Her dry
 lips could not speak
 the “No” which I saw rather than
4955𓀴      heard.
4956𓀴
4957𓀴      “Surely your
 memory deceives
 you,” said I. “I could even quote a
4958𓀴      passage of your letter. It
 ran ‘Please, please, as you are a
4959𓀴      gentleman,
 burn this letter,
 and be at the gate by ten o’clock.’”
4960𓀴
4961𓀴      I thought that
 she had fainted,
 but she recovered herself by a
4962𓀴      supreme effort.
4963𓀴
4964𓀴      “Is there no such thing
 as a gentleman?” she gasped.
4965𓀴
4966𓀴      “You do Sir
 Charles an injustice.
 He _did_ burn the letter. But
4967𓀴      sometimes a letter may
 be legible even when burned. You
4968𓀴      acknowledge now
 that you wrote it?”
4969𓀴
4970𓀴      “Yes, I did write it,” she
 cried, pouring out her soul in a
4971𓀴      torrent of
 words. “I did write
 it. Why should I deny it? I have
4972𓀴      no reason to be ashamed
 of it. I wished him to help me. I
4973𓀴      believed that
 if I had an interview
 I could gain his help, so I
4974𓀴      asked him to meet me.”
4975𓀴
4976𓀴      “But why at such an hour?”
4977𓀴
4978𓀴      “Because I had
 only just learned
 that he was going to London next
4979𓀴      day and might
 be away for months.
 There were reasons why I could
4980𓀴      not get there earlier.”
4981𓀴
4982𓀴      “But why a
 rendezvous in the
 garden instead of a visit to the
4983𓀴      house?”
4984𓀴
4985𓀴      “Do you think a woman could
 go alone at that hour to a bachelor’s
4986𓀴      house?”
4987𓀴
4988𓀴      “Well, what happened
 when you did get there?”
4989𓀴
4990𓀴      “I never went.”
4991𓀴
4992𓀴      “Mrs. Lyons!”
4993𓀴
4994𓀴      “No, I swear it to you on
 all I hold sacred. I never went.
4995𓀴      Something intervened
 to prevent my going.”
4996𓀴
4997𓀴      “What was that?”
4998𓀴
4999𓀴      “That is a private
 matter. I cannot tell it.”
5000𓀴
5001𓀴      “You acknowledge then that
 you made an appointment with Sir
5002𓀴      Charles at
 the very hour and
 place at which he met his death, but
5003𓀴      you deny that you
 kept the appointment.”
5004𓀴
5005𓀴      “That is the truth.”
5006𓀴
5007𓀴      Again and again
 I cross-questioned
 her, but I could never get
5008𓀴      past that point.
5009𓀴
5010𓀴      “Mrs. Lyons,”
 said I as I rose
 from this long and inconclusive
5011𓀴      interview, “you are taking
 a very great responsibility and
5012𓀴      putting yourself in a very
 false position by not making an
5013𓀴      absolutely clean breast of
 all that you know. If I have to call
5014𓀴      in the aid
 of the police you
 will find how seriously you are
5015𓀴      compromised.
 If your position
 is innocent, why did you in the
5016𓀴      first instance deny having
 written to Sir Charles upon that
5017𓀴      date?”
5018𓀴
5019𓀴      “Because I feared that some
 false conclusion might be drawn from
5020𓀴      it and that I might find
 myself involved in a scandal.”
5021𓀴
5022𓀴      “And why were
 you so pressing
 that Sir Charles should destroy
5023𓀴      your letter?”
5024𓀴
5025𓀴      “If you have read
 the letter you will know.”
5026𓀴
5027𓀴      “I did not say that
 I had read all the letter.”
5028𓀴
5029𓀴      “You quoted some of it.”
5030𓀴
5031𓀴      “I quoted
 the postscript. The
 letter had, as I said, been burned
5032𓀴      and it was not all legible.
 I ask you once again why it was that
5033𓀴      you were so
 pressing that Sir
 Charles should destroy this letter
5034𓀴      which he received
 on the day of his death.”
5035𓀴
5036𓀴      “The matter is
 a very private one.”
5037𓀴
5038𓀴      “The more reason
 why you should
 avoid a public investigation.”
5039𓀴
5040𓀴      “I will tell
 you, then. If you
 have heard anything of my unhappy
5041𓀴      history you
 will know that I
 made a rash marriage and had reason
5042𓀴      to regret it.”
5043𓀴
5044𓀴      “I have heard so much.”
5045𓀴
5046𓀴      “My life has
 been one incessant
 persecution from a husband whom I
5047𓀴      abhor. The law
 is upon his side,
 and every day I am faced by the
5048𓀴      possibility
 that he may force
 me to live with him. At the time
5049𓀴      that I wrote this letter to
 Sir Charles I had learned that there
5050𓀴      was a prospect
 of my regaining
 my freedom if certain expenses
5051𓀴      could be met.
 It meant everything
 to me—peace of mind, happiness,
5052𓀴      self-respect—everything. I
 knew Sir Charles’s generosity, and I
5053𓀴      thought that
 if he heard the
 story from my own lips he would help
5054𓀴      me.”
5055𓀴
5056𓀴      “Then how is it
 that you did not go?”
5057𓀴
5058𓀴      “Because I received help in
 the interval from another source.”
5059𓀴
5060𓀴      “Why then,
 did you not write
 to Sir Charles and explain this?”
5061𓀴
5062𓀴      “So I should
 have done had I
 not seen his death in the paper next
5063𓀴      morning.”
5064𓀴
5065𓀴      The woman’s
 story hung coherently
 together, and all my questions
5066𓀴      were unable
 to shake it. I could
 only check it by finding if she
5067𓀴      had, indeed,
 instituted divorce
 proceedings against her husband
5068𓀴      at or about the
 time of the tragedy.
5069𓀴
5070𓀴      It was unlikely
 that she would
 dare to say that she had not been
5071𓀴      to Baskerville Hall if she
 really had been, for a trap would be
5072𓀴      necessary
 to take her there,
 and could not have returned to
5073𓀴      Coombe Tracey
 until the early
 hours of the morning. Such an
5074𓀴      excursion could not be kept
 secret. The probability was,
5075𓀴      therefore,
 that she was telling
 the truth, or, at least, a part
5076𓀴      of the truth.
 I came away baffled
 and disheartened. Once again I
5077𓀴      had reached
 that dead wall which
 seemed to be built across every
5078𓀴      path by which
 I tried to get
 at the object of my mission. And yet
5079𓀴      the more I thought
 of the lady’s
 face and of her manner the more
5080𓀴      I felt that
 something was being
 held back from me. Why should she
5081𓀴      turn so pale?
 Why should she
 fight against every admission until
5082𓀴      it was forced from her? Why
 should she have been so reticent at
5083𓀴      the time of
 the tragedy? Surely
 the explanation of all this could
5084𓀴      not be as innocent
 as she would
 have me believe. For the moment I
5085𓀴      could proceed no farther in
 that direction, but must turn back to
5086𓀴      that other
 clue which was to
 be sought for among the stone huts
5087𓀴      upon the moor.
5088𓀴
5089𓀴      And that was a most vague
 direction. I realised it as I drove
5090𓀴      back and noted
 how hill after
 hill showed traces of the ancient
5091𓀴      people. Barrymore’s
 only indication
 had been that the stranger
5092𓀴      lived in one
 of these abandoned
 huts, and many hundreds of them
5093𓀴      are scattered
 throughout the
 length and breadth of the moor. But
5094𓀴      I had my own experience for
 a guide since it had shown me the man
5095𓀴      himself standing
 upon the summit
 of the Black Tor. That, then,
5096𓀴      should be the centre of my
 search. From there I should explore
5097𓀴      every hut
 upon the moor until
 I lighted upon the right one. If
5098𓀴      this man were
 inside it I should
 find out from his own lips, at
5099𓀴      the point of my revolver if
 necessary, who he was and why he had
5100𓀴      dogged us so long. He might
 slip away from us in the crowd of
5101𓀴      Regent Street, but it would
 puzzle him to do so upon the lonely
5102𓀴      moor. On the other hand, if
 I should find the hut and its tenant
5103𓀴      should not be within it I
 must remain there, however long the
5104𓀴      vigil, until he returned.
 Holmes had missed him in London. It
5105𓀴      would indeed be a triumph
 for me if I could run him to earth
5106𓀴      where my master had failed.
5107𓀴
5108𓀴      Luck had been
 against us again
 and again in this inquiry, but now
5109𓀴      at last it
 came to my aid. And
 the messenger of good fortune was
5110𓀴      none other
 than Mr. Frankland,
 who was standing, grey-whiskered
5111𓀴      and red-faced,
 outside the gate
 of his garden, which opened on to
5112𓀴      the highroad along
 which I travelled.
5113𓀴
5114𓀴      “Good-day,
 Dr. Watson,” cried
 he with unwonted good humour, “you
5115𓀴      must really
 give your horses
 a rest and come in to have a glass
5116𓀴      of wine and
 to congratulate me.”
5117𓀴
5118𓀴      My feelings
 towards him were
 very far from being friendly after
5119𓀴      what I had heard
 of his treatment
 of his daughter, but I was
5120𓀴      anxious to send Perkins
 and the wagonette home, and the
5121𓀴      opportunity was a good one.
 I alighted and sent a message to Sir
5122𓀴      Henry that
 I should walk over
 in time for dinner. Then I followed
5123𓀴      Frankland into
 his dining-room.
5124𓀴

5127𓀴      event. I mean to teach them

5128𓀴      that there

5129𓀴      established a

5138𓀴      had Sir John

5220𓀴      would be likely to take his

5222𓀴

5255𓀴

5375𓀴
5376𓀴
5377𓀴
5378𓀴
5379𓀴Chapter 12.
5380𓀴Death on the Moor
5381𓀴

5506𓀴      upon the zeal

5507𓀴      extraordinarily

5902𓀴      “We have been expecting you

5903𓀴      down. You are in
 time to see a tragedy.”
5904𓀴
5905𓀴      “Yes, indeed.
 I have no doubt
 that my friend’s explanation will
5906𓀴      cover the
 facts. I will take
 an unpleasant remembrance back to
5907𓀴      London with me tomorrow.”
5908𓀴
5909𓀴      “Oh, you return tomorrow?”
5910𓀴
5911𓀴      “That is my intention.”
5912𓀴
5913𓀴      “I hope your visit has cast
 some light upon those occurrences
5914𓀴      which have puzzled us?”
5915𓀴
5916𓀴      Holmes shrugged
 his shoulders.
5917𓀴
5918𓀴      “One cannot always have the
 success for which one hopes. An
5919𓀴      investigator
 needs facts and
 not legends or rumours. It has not
5920𓀴      been a satisfactory case.”
5921𓀴
5922𓀴      My friend spoke
 in his frankest
 and most unconcerned manner.
5923𓀴      Stapleton still looked hard
 at him. Then he turned to me.
5924𓀴
5925𓀴      “I would suggest
 carrying this
 poor fellow to my house, but it
5926𓀴      would give my sister such a
 fright that I do not feel justified
5927𓀴      in doing it.
 I think that if
 we put something over his face he
5928𓀴      will be safe
 until morning.”
5929𓀴
5930𓀴      And so it was arranged.
 Resisting Stapleton’s offer of
5931𓀴      hospitality,
 Holmes and I set
 off to Baskerville Hall, leaving
5932𓀴      the naturalist

 Looking back we saw the figure
5933𓀴      moving slowly away over the
 broad moor, and behind him that one
5934𓀴      black smudge
 on the silvered
 slope which showed where the man was
5935𓀴      lying who had come
 so horribly to his end.
5936𓀴
5937𓀴      “We’re at close
 grips at last,”
 said Holmes as we walked together
5938𓀴      across the
 moor. “What a nerve
 the fellow has! How he pulled
5939𓀴      himself together
 in the face

5940𓀴      shock when
 he found that the
 wrong man had fallen a victim to his
5941𓀴      plot. I told you in London,
 Watson, and I tell you now again,
5942𓀴      that we have never had a
 foeman more worthy of our steel.”
5943𓀴
5944𓀴      “I am sorry that
 he has seen you.”
5945𓀴
5946𓀴      “And so was I at first. But
 there was no getting out of it.”
5947𓀴
5948𓀴      “What effect
 do you think it
 will have upon his plans now that he
5949𓀴      knows you are here?”
5950𓀴
5951𓀴      “It may cause
 him to be more
 cautious, or it may drive him to
5952𓀴      desperate measures at once.
 Like most clever criminals, he may be
5953𓀴      too confident in his own
 cleverness and imagine that he has
5954𓀴      completely deceived us.”
5955𓀴
5956𓀴      “Why should we
 not arrest him at once?”
5957𓀴
5958𓀴      “My dear Watson, you were
 born to be a man of action. Your
5959𓀴      instinct is always
 to do something
 energetic. But supposing, for
5960𓀴      argument’s
 sake, that we had
 him arrested tonight, what on earth
5961𓀴      the better off should we be
 for that? We could prove nothing
5962𓀴      against him. There’s the
 devilish cunning of it! If he were
5963𓀴      acting through
 a human agent
 we could get some evidence, but if
5964𓀴      we were to drag this great
 dog to the light of day it would not
5965𓀴      help us in putting a rope
 round the neck of its master.”
5966𓀴
5967𓀴      “Surely we have a case.”
5968𓀴
5969𓀴      “Not a shadow
 of one—only surmise
 and conjecture. We should be
5970𓀴      laughed out of court if we
 came with such a story and such
5971𓀴      evidence.”
5972𓀴
5973𓀴      “There is Sir
 Charles’s death.”
5974𓀴
5975𓀴      “Found dead
 without a mark upon
 him. You and I know that he died
5976𓀴      of sheer fright,
 and we know
 also what frightened him, but how
5977𓀴      are we to get twelve stolid
 jurymen to know it? What signs are
5978𓀴      there of a hound? Where are

5979𓀴      know that a
 hound does not bite

5980𓀴      was dead before
 ever the brute
 overtook him. But we have to prove
5981𓀴      all this, and we are
 not in a position to do it.”
5982𓀴
5983𓀴      “Well, then, tonight?”
5984𓀴
5985𓀴      “We are not much better off
 tonight. Again, there was no direct
5986𓀴      connection
 between the hound
 and the man’s death. We never saw
5987𓀴      the hound. We heard it, but
 we could not prove that it was
5988𓀴      running upon
 this man’s trail.
 There is a complete absence of
5989𓀴      motive. No, my dear fellow;
 we must reconcile ourselves to the
5990𓀴      fact that
 we have no case at
 present, and that it is worth our
5991𓀴      while to run any risk

5992𓀴
5993𓀴      “And how do you
 propose to do so?”
5994𓀴
5995𓀴      “I have great hopes of what
 Mrs. Laura Lyons may do for us when
5996𓀴      the position of affairs is
 made clear to her. And I have my own
5997𓀴      plan as well.
 Sufficient for
 tomorrow is the evil thereof; but I
5998𓀴      hope before the day is past
 to have the upper hand at last.”
5999𓀴
6000𓀴      I could draw

6001𓀴      thought, as far
 as the Baskerville gates.
6002𓀴
6003𓀴      “Are you coming up?”
6004𓀴
6005𓀴      “Yes; I see

6006𓀴      Watson. Say

6007𓀴      Selden’s death

6008𓀴      have a better nerve for the
 ordeal which he will have to undergo
6009𓀴      tomorrow,

6010𓀴      to dine with these people.”
6011𓀴
6012𓀴      “And so am I.”
6013𓀴
6014𓀴      “Then you must
 excuse yourself

6015𓀴      easily arranged.
 And now, if
 we are too late for dinner, I think
6016𓀴      that we are both
 ready for our suppers.”
6017𓀴
6018𓀴
6019𓀴
6020𓀴
6021𓀴Chapter 13.
6022𓀴Fixing the Nets
6023𓀴
6024𓀴
6025𓀴      Sir Henry was
 more pleased than
 surprised to see Sherlock Holmes,
6026𓀴      for he had
 for some days been
 expecting that recent events would
6027𓀴      bring him down from London.
 He did raise his eyebrows, however,
6028𓀴      when he found
 that my friend

6029𓀴      explanations

 Between us we soon supplied his
6030𓀴      wants, and

6031𓀴      as much of

 it seemed desirable that he should
6032𓀴      know. But first

6033𓀴      Barrymore and his wife. To

6034𓀴      relief, but

6035𓀴      was the man

6036𓀴      he always

6037𓀴      child who had clung to her

6038𓀴      not one woman to mourn him.
6039𓀴
6040𓀴      “I’ve been

6041𓀴      the morning,”

6042𓀴      credit, for I have kept my

6043𓀴      about alone

6044𓀴      message from Stapleton

6045𓀴
6046𓀴      “I have no
 doubt that you would
 have had a more lively evening,”
6047𓀴      said Holmes drily. “By the

6048𓀴      that we have
 been mourning over
 you as having broken your neck?”
6049𓀴
6050𓀴      Sir Henry opened

6051𓀴
6052𓀴      “This poor

 in your clothes. I fear your
6053𓀴      servant who gave them to

6054𓀴      police.”
6055𓀴
6056𓀴      “That is unlikely.
 There was

6057𓀴      know.”
6058𓀴
6059𓀴      “That’s lucky

6060𓀴      you are all
 on the wrong side

6061𓀴      sure that

6062𓀴      arrest the whole household.

6063𓀴      incriminating documents.”
6064𓀴
6065𓀴      “But how about the case?”
 asked the baronet. “Have you made
6066𓀴      anything out of the tangle?
 I don’t know that Watson and I are
6067𓀴      much the wiser

6068𓀴
6069𓀴      “I think that I shall be in
 a position to make the situation
6070𓀴      rather more

6071𓀴      difficult and

6072𓀴      upon which we

6073𓀴
6074𓀴      “We’ve had one experience,
 as Watson has no doubt told you. We
6075𓀴      heard the

6076𓀴      empty superstition.
 I had something
 to do with dogs when I was
6077𓀴      out West,
 and I know one when
 I hear one. If you can muzzle that
6078𓀴      one and put him on a chain

6079𓀴      greatest detective

6080𓀴
6081𓀴      “I think I will muzzle him
 and chain him all right if you will
6082𓀴      give me your help.”
6083𓀴
6084𓀴      “Whatever you tell
 me to do I will do.”
6085𓀴
6086𓀴      “Very good; and I will ask
 you also to do it blindly, without
6087𓀴      always asking the reason.”
6088𓀴
6089𓀴      “Just as you like.”
6090𓀴
6091𓀴      “If you will
 do this I think
 the chances are that our little
6092𓀴      problem will soon be
 solved. I have no doubt—”
6093𓀴
6094𓀴      He stopped

6095𓀴      air. The lamp beat upon his

6096𓀴      still that it might have

6097𓀴      statue, a personification

6098𓀴
6099𓀴      “What is it?”
 we both cried.
6100𓀴
6101𓀴      I could see as he looked

6102𓀴      internal emotion.
 His features
 were still composed, but his eyes
6103𓀴      shone with

6104𓀴
6105𓀴      “Excuse the admiration of a

6106𓀴      hand towards

6107𓀴      wall. “Watson
 won’t allow that
 I know anything of art but that is
6108𓀴      mere jealousy because our

6109𓀴      these are a really very

6110𓀴
6111𓀴      “Well, I’m glad to hear you

6112𓀴      with some surprise

 “I don’t pretend to know much
6113𓀴      about these things, and I’d

6114𓀴      steer than of a picture. I

6115𓀴      such things.”
6116𓀴
6117𓀴      “I know what is good when I

6118𓀴      Kneller, I’ll

6119𓀴      the stout

6120𓀴      all family portraits,

6121𓀴

6123𓀴

6125𓀴
6126𓀴      “Barrymore

6127𓀴      lessons fairly well.”
6128𓀴

6131𓀴      “That is Rear-Admiral

6132𓀴      West Indies.

6133𓀴      Sir William

6134𓀴      House of Commons

6135𓀴

6137𓀴      and the lace?”
6138𓀴
6139𓀴      “Ah, you have
 a right to know

6140𓀴      the mischief, the wicked

6141𓀴      Baskervilles. We’re

6142𓀴
6143𓀴      I gazed with interest and

6144𓀴
6145𓀴      “Dear me!” said Holmes, “he
 seems a quiet, meek-mannered man
6146𓀴      enough, but I dare say that

6147𓀴      eyes. I had pictured him as

6148𓀴
6149𓀴      “There’s no doubt about the

6150𓀴      date, 1647, are on

6151𓀴
6152𓀴      Holmes said

6153𓀴      seemed to

6154𓀴      continually

6155𓀴      when Sir Henry had gone to

6157𓀴      banqueting-hall,

6159𓀴
6160𓀴      “Do you see

6161𓀴

6169𓀴

6171𓀴

6173𓀴      a chair, and,

6175𓀴
6176𓀴      “Good heavens!”

6177𓀴
6178𓀴      The face of Stapleton

6179𓀴
6180𓀴      “Ha, you see

6181𓀴      and not their trimmings. It

6182𓀴      investigator that he should

6183𓀴
6184𓀴      “But this is marvellous.

6185𓀴
6186𓀴      “Yes, it is an

6187𓀴      to be both physical

6188𓀴      enough to convert a man to

6189𓀴      fellow is

6190𓀴

6194𓀴      our most obvious

6195𓀴      and I dare

6196𓀴      in our net

6199𓀴      from the picture.

6200𓀴      always boded

6201𓀴
6202𓀴      I was up betimes

6208𓀴      is out whether

6209𓀴      whether he has

6210𓀴
6211𓀴      “Have you been

6212𓀴

6221𓀴

6298𓀴

6300𓀴

6313𓀴      waiting upon the platform.
6314𓀴
6315𓀴      “Any orders, sir?”
6316𓀴

6318𓀴      arrive you will send a wire

6319𓀴      to say that if he finds the

6320𓀴      to send it by registered

6343𓀴      drawing closer around

6452𓀴      should always

6473𓀴

6490𓀴
6491𓀴
6492𓀴
6493𓀴
6494𓀴Chapter 14.
6495𓀴The Hound of the Baskervilles
6496𓀴
6497𓀴
6498𓀴      One of Sherlock
 Holmes’s defects—if,
 indeed, one may call it a
6499𓀴      defect—was that
 he was exceedingly
 loath to communicate his full
6500𓀴      plans to any
 other person until
 the instant of their fulfilment.
6501𓀴      Partly it
 came no doubt from
 his own masterful nature, which
6502𓀴      loved to dominate
 and surprise
 those who were around him. Partly
6503𓀴      also from his
 professional caution,
 which urged him never to take
6504𓀴      any chances.
 The result, however,
 was very trying for those who
6505𓀴      were acting
 as his agents and
 assistants. I had often suffered
6506𓀴      under it, but never more so
 than during that long drive in the
6507𓀴      darkness. The great ordeal
 was in front of us; at last we were
6508𓀴      about to make
 our final effort,
 and yet Holmes had said nothing,
6509𓀴      and I could
 only surmise what
 his course of action would be. My
6510𓀴      nerves thrilled
 with anticipation
 when at last the cold wind upon
6511𓀴      our faces
 and the dark, void

6512𓀴      road told me
 that we were back
 upon the moor once again. Every
6513𓀴      stride of the
 horses and every
 turn of the wheels was taking us
6514𓀴      nearer to our
 supreme adventure.
6515𓀴
6516𓀴      Our conversation
 was hampered
 by the presence of the driver of
6517𓀴      the hired
 wagonette, so that
 we were forced to talk of trivial
6518𓀴      matters when
 our nerves were
 tense with emotion and anticipation.
6519𓀴      It was a relief
 to me, after
 that unnatural restraint, when we at
6520𓀴      last passed
 Frankland’s house
 and knew that we were drawing near
6521𓀴      to the Hall
 and to the scene
 of action. We did not drive up to
6522𓀴      the door but
 got down near the
 gate of the avenue. The wagonette
6523𓀴      was paid off and ordered to
 return to Coombe Tracey forthwith,
6524𓀴      while we started
 to walk to Merripit House.
6525𓀴
6526𓀴      “Are you armed, Lestrade?”
6527𓀴
6528𓀴      The little
 detective smiled.
 “As long as I have my trousers I
6529𓀴      have a hip-pocket, and as
 long as I have my hip-pocket I have
6530𓀴      something in it.”
6531𓀴
6532𓀴      “Good! My friend and I
 are also ready for emergencies.”
6533𓀴
6534𓀴      “You’re mighty close about
 this affair, Mr. Holmes. What’s the
6535𓀴      game now?”
6536𓀴
6537𓀴      “A waiting game.”
6538𓀴
6539𓀴      “My word, it does not seem
 a very cheerful place,” said the
6540𓀴      detective with

6541𓀴      of the hill and at the huge

6542𓀴      Grimpen Mire. “I see the

6543𓀴
6544𓀴      “That is Merripit House and

6545𓀴      request you

6546𓀴
6547𓀴      We moved cautiously

6548𓀴      house, but Holmes halted us

6549𓀴      from it.
6550𓀴
6551𓀴      “This will

6552𓀴      admirable screen.”
6553𓀴
6554𓀴      “We are to wait here?”
6555𓀴
6556𓀴      “Yes, we shall

6557𓀴      Lestrade. You

6558𓀴      Can you tell

6559𓀴      windows at this end?”
6560𓀴
6561𓀴      “I think they are

6562𓀴
6563𓀴      “And the one beyond,
 which shines so brightly?”
6564𓀴
6565𓀴      “That is certainly

6566𓀴
6567𓀴      “The blinds
 are up. You know

6568𓀴      forward quietly

6569𓀴      don’t let them know

6570𓀴
6571𓀴      I tiptoed down the path and

6572𓀴      surrounded

6573𓀴      a point whence I could look

6574𓀴      window.
6575𓀴
6576𓀴      There were only two men in

6577𓀴      They sat with

6578𓀴      round table. Both of them

6579𓀴      wine were in front of them.

6580𓀴      but the baronet looked pale

6581𓀴      that lonely walk across the

6582𓀴      upon his mind.
6583𓀴

6585𓀴      Henry filled

6586𓀴      puffing at

6588𓀴      the other side

6590𓀴      corner of

6591𓀴      in there was

6592𓀴      minute or

6593𓀴      he passed me

6594𓀴      and I crept quietly back to

6595𓀴      tell them what I had seen.
6596𓀴
6597𓀴      “You say, Watson,

 is not there?” Holmes asked when
6598𓀴      I had finished my report.
6599𓀴
6600𓀴      “No.”
6601𓀴
6602𓀴      “Where can
 she be, then, since
 there is no light in any other
6603𓀴      room except the kitchen?”
6604𓀴
6605𓀴      “I cannot think
 where she is.”
6606𓀴
6607𓀴      I have said
 that over the great

6608𓀴      white fog. It was drifting

6609𓀴      itself up

6610𓀴      defined. The moon shone

6611𓀴      shimmering

6613𓀴      he muttered impatiently

6616𓀴
6617𓀴      “Is that serious?”
6618𓀴
6619𓀴      “Very serious,

6620𓀴      disarranged

6621𓀴      ten o’clock.

6623𓀴
6624𓀴      The night

6625𓀴      bright, while a half-moon

6626𓀴      uncertain light. Before us

6627𓀴      serrated roof and bristling

6629𓀴      windows stretched

6631𓀴      only remained

6632𓀴      murderous host

6633𓀴      their cigars.
6634𓀴
6635𓀴      Every minute

6636𓀴      the moor was

6642𓀴      which the

6645𓀴

6647𓀴      covered. In half an hour we

6649𓀴
6650𓀴      “Shall we move farther

6651𓀴
6652𓀴      “Yes, I think

6653𓀴

6655𓀴      were half a

6656𓀴      with the moon silvering

6660𓀴      of his being

6661𓀴      must hold our ground where

6662𓀴      clapped his

6663𓀴      him coming.”
6664𓀴

6666𓀴      among the stones

6667𓀴      front of us. The steps grew

6669𓀴      He looked round

6671𓀴      to where we

6672𓀴      walked he

6673𓀴      who is ill at ease.
6674𓀴

6677𓀴
6678𓀴      There was a thin,

6679𓀴      heart of that

 The cloud was within fifty yards of
6680𓀴      where we lay, and we glared

6681𓀴      horror was

6682𓀴      elbow, and I glanced for an

6684𓀴      suddenly they

6686𓀴      yell of terror

6687𓀴      sprang to my feet, my inert

6688𓀴      paralyzed by

6689𓀴      the shadows

6691𓀴      burst from its open mouth,

6693𓀴      flickering flame. Never in

6694𓀴      brain could

6695𓀴      conceived

6696𓀴      out of the wall of fog.
6697𓀴

6699𓀴      track, following hard upon

6700𓀴      paralyzed were

6704𓀴      onward. Far
 away on the path
 we saw Sir Henry looking back, his
6705𓀴      face white
 in the moonlight,
 his hands raised in horror, glaring
6706𓀴      helplessly at the frightful
 thing which was hunting him down. But
6707𓀴      that cry of
 pain from the hound
 had blown all our fears to the
6708𓀴      winds. If he was vulnerable
 he was mortal, and if we could wound
6709𓀴      him we could
 kill him. Never
 have I seen a man run as Holmes ran
6710𓀴      that night.
 I am reckoned fleet
 of foot, but he outpaced me as
6711𓀴      much as I
 outpaced the little
 professional. In front of us as we
6712𓀴      flew up the
 track we heard scream
 after scream from Sir Henry and
6713𓀴      the deep roar of the hound.
 I was in time to see the beast spring
6714𓀴      upon its victim,
 hurl him to
 the ground, and worry at his throat.
6715𓀴      But the next instant Holmes
 had emptied five barrels of his
6716𓀴      revolver into

6717𓀴      a vicious snap in the air,

6718𓀴      pawing furiously, and then

6719𓀴      panting, and
 pressed my pistol
 to the dreadful, shimmering head,
6720𓀴      but it was useless to press
 the trigger. The giant hound was
6721𓀴      dead.
6722𓀴
6723𓀴      Sir Henry lay
 insensible where

6724𓀴      collar, and Holmes breathed

6725𓀴      that there was no sign of a

6726𓀴      time. Already

6727𓀴      effort to move.
 Lestrade thrust

6728𓀴      baronet’s teeth,
 and two frightened

6729𓀴
6730𓀴      “My God!”
 he whispered. “What
 was it? What, in heaven’s name, was
6731𓀴      it?”
6732𓀴
6733𓀴      “It’s dead,
 whatever it is,”
 said Holmes. “We’ve laid the family
6734𓀴      ghost once and forever.”
6735𓀴
6736𓀴      In mere size
 and strength it
 was a terrible creature which was
6737𓀴      lying stretched before us.
 It was not a pure bloodhound and it
6738𓀴      was not a pure mastiff; but

6739𓀴      the two—gaunt,

6740𓀴      in the stillness of death,

6742𓀴      ringed with fire. I placed

6743𓀴      as I held them

6744𓀴      darkness.
6745𓀴
6746𓀴      “Phosphorus,” I said.
6747𓀴
6748𓀴      “A cunning
 preparation of it,”
 said Holmes, sniffing at the dead
6749𓀴      animal. “There is no smell

6750𓀴      power of scent.

6751𓀴      exposed you to this fright.

6752𓀴      for such a

6753𓀴      receive him.”
6754𓀴
6755𓀴      “You have saved my life.”
6756𓀴
6757𓀴      “Having first
 endangered it.
 Are you strong enough to stand?”
6758𓀴
6759𓀴      “Give me another
 mouthful of
 that brandy and I shall be ready for
6760𓀴      anything. So!
 Now, if you will

6761𓀴      do?”
6762𓀴
6763𓀴      “To leave you here. You are
 not fit for further adventures
6764𓀴      tonight. If you will wait,

6765𓀴      you to the Hall.”
6766𓀴
6767𓀴      He tried to stagger to his
 feet; but he was still ghastly pale
6768𓀴      and trembling

 We helped him to a rock, where he
6769𓀴      sat shivering with his

6770𓀴
6771𓀴      “We must leave

6772𓀴      be done, and

6773𓀴      now we only want our man.
6774𓀴
6775𓀴      “It’s a thousand

6776𓀴      continued

6777𓀴      shots must have told

6778𓀴
6779𓀴      “We were some distance off,

6780𓀴
6781𓀴      “He followed the hound to

6782𓀴      certain. No, no, he’s gone

6783𓀴      house and make sure.”
6784𓀴
6785𓀴      The front door was open, so

6786𓀴      room to the amazement of a

6787𓀴      in the passage.
 There was no

6788𓀴      Holmes caught up the lamp

6789𓀴      unexplored.
 No sign could we

6790𓀴      On the upper

6791𓀴
6792𓀴      “There’s someone in here,”
 cried Lestrade. “I can hear a
6793𓀴      movement. Open this door!”
6794𓀴
6795𓀴      A faint moaning
 and rustling

6796𓀴      door just

6797𓀴      open. Pistol in hand, we

6798𓀴

6800𓀴      villain whom we expected to

6801𓀴      object so strange

6802𓀴      staring at it in amazement.
6803𓀴
6804𓀴      The room had been fashioned

6805𓀴      were lined by a number of

6806𓀴      collection

6807𓀴      been the relaxation of this

6808𓀴      centre of this room there

6809𓀴      placed at some period as a

6810𓀴      of timber which spanned the

6811𓀴      so swathed and muffled in

6812𓀴      secure it

6813𓀴      that of a

6814𓀴      was secured

6815𓀴      part of the face, and over

6817𓀴      minute we had torn off the

6818𓀴      Stapleton

6819𓀴      head fell upon her chest I

6820𓀴      across her neck.
6821𓀴
6822𓀴      “The brute!” cried Holmes.
 “Here, Lestrade, your brandy-bottle!
6823𓀴      Put her in the chair! She
 has fainted from ill-usage and
6824𓀴      exhaustion.”
6825𓀴
6826𓀴      She opened her eyes again.
6827𓀴
6828𓀴      “Is he safe?” she

6829𓀴
6830𓀴      “He cannot
 escape us, madam.”
6831𓀴
6832𓀴      “No, no, I did not mean my
 husband. Sir Henry? Is he safe?”
6833𓀴
6834𓀴      “Yes.”
6835𓀴
6836𓀴      “And the hound?”
6837𓀴
6838𓀴      “It is dead.”
6839𓀴
6840𓀴      She gave a long

6841𓀴
6842𓀴      “Thank God! Thank God! Oh,

6843𓀴      me!” She shot her arms out

6844𓀴      horror that they were all

6845𓀴      nothing—nothing!
 It is my mind

6846𓀴      defiled. I could endure it

6847𓀴      deception,

6848𓀴      that I had

6849𓀴      his dupe and his tool.” She

6850𓀴      spoke.
6851𓀴
6852𓀴      “You bear him no good will,
 madam,” said Holmes. “Tell us then
6853𓀴      where we shall find him. If
 you have ever aided him in evil, help
6854𓀴      us now and so atone.”
6855𓀴
6856𓀴      “There is

6857𓀴      “There is
 an old tin mine on

6858𓀴      It was there

6859𓀴      preparations

6860𓀴      would fly.”
6861𓀴
6862𓀴      The fog-bank lay like white
 wool against the window. Holmes held
6863𓀴      the lamp towards it.
6864𓀴
6865𓀴      “See,” said

6866𓀴      tonight.”
6867𓀴
6868𓀴      She laughed and clapped her

6869𓀴      with fierce merriment.
6870𓀴
6871𓀴      “He may find
 his way in, but

6872𓀴      see the guiding

 We planted them together, he and
6873𓀴      I, to mark

6874𓀴      plucked them

6875𓀴      your mercy!”
6876𓀴
6877𓀴      It was evident
 to us that all

6878𓀴      had lifted.
 Meanwhile we left

6879𓀴      while Holmes

6880𓀴      Hall. The story
 of the Stapletons
 could no longer be withheld
6881𓀴      from him, but
 he took the blow

6882𓀴      about the woman whom he had
 loved. But the shock of the night’s
6883𓀴      adventures
 had shattered his

6884𓀴      delirious in

6885𓀴      of them were
 destined to travel
 together round the world before
6886𓀴      Sir Henry had
 become once more
 the hale, hearty man that he had
6887𓀴      been before

6888𓀴
6889𓀴      And now I come rapidly to
 the conclusion of this singular
6890𓀴      narrative, in which I have
 tried to make the reader share those
6891𓀴      dark fears
 and vague surmises

6892𓀴      ended in so

 On the morning after the death of
6893𓀴      the hound
 the fog had lifted

6894𓀴      to the point where they had

6895𓀴      helped us to

6896𓀴      the eagerness and joy with

6897𓀴      track. We left her standing

6898𓀴      peaty soil

6899𓀴      end of it a

6900𓀴      path zigzagged from tuft

6901𓀴      green-scummed pits and foul

6902𓀴      stranger. Rank

6903𓀴      of decay and

6904𓀴      false step plunged us more

6905𓀴      quivering mire, which shook

6906𓀴      our feet.
 Its tenacious grip

6907𓀴      and when we sank into it it

6908𓀴      tugging us down

6909𓀴      was the clutch

6910𓀴      someone had
 passed that perilous

6911𓀴      of cotton

6912𓀴      was projecting. Holmes sank

6913𓀴      path to seize

6914𓀴      could never

6915𓀴      old black boot

6916𓀴      leather inside.
6917𓀴
6918𓀴      “It is worth

6919𓀴      missing boot.”
6920𓀴
6921𓀴      “Thrown there by
 Stapleton in his flight.”
6922𓀴
6923𓀴      “Exactly. He retained it in
 his hand after using it to set the
6924𓀴      hound upon

6925𓀴      clutching it. And he hurled

6926𓀴      We know at least that

6927𓀴
6928𓀴      But more than that we were

6929𓀴      was much which

 There was no chance of finding
6930𓀴      footsteps in the mire, for

6931𓀴      them, but as

6932𓀴      we all looked eagerly for

6933𓀴      ever met our

6934𓀴      never reached

6935𓀴      through the

6936𓀴      the great

6937𓀴      which had sucked him in,

6938𓀴      forever buried.
6939𓀴
6940𓀴      Many traces we found of him

6941𓀴      hid his savage ally. A huge

6942𓀴      with rubbish

6943𓀴      were the crumbling

6944𓀴      away no doubt

6945𓀴      of these a staple and chain

6946𓀴      showed where the animal had

6947𓀴      tangle of

6948𓀴

6950𓀴      Mortimer will never see his

6951𓀴      this place contains any

6952𓀴      fathomed. He could hide his

6953𓀴      voice, and hence came those

6954𓀴      pleasant to

6955𓀴      out-house

6956𓀴      on the supreme day, which

6957𓀴      efforts, that
 he dared do it.
 This paste in the tin is no doubt
6958𓀴      the luminous
 mixture with which
 the creature was daubed. It was
6959𓀴      suggested,
 of course, by the
 story of the family hell-hound, and
6960𓀴      by the desire
 to frighten old

6961𓀴      poor devil of a convict ran

6962𓀴      and as we ourselves

6963𓀴      bounding through

6964𓀴      a cunning

6965𓀴      victim to his

6967𓀴      have done, upon the moor? I

6968𓀴      it again now,

6969𓀴      dangerous

6970𓀴      towards the

6971𓀴      stretched away

6972𓀴      moor.
6973𓀴
6974𓀴
6975𓀴
6976𓀴
6977𓀴Chapter 15.
6978𓀴A Retrospection
6979𓀴
6980𓀴
6981𓀴      It was the end of November,
 and Holmes and I sat, upon a raw and
6982𓀴      foggy night, on either side
 of a blazing fire in our sitting-room
6983𓀴      in Baker Street. Since the
 tragic upshot of our visit to
6984𓀴      Devonshire
 he had been engaged
 in two affairs of the utmost
6985𓀴      importance, in the first of
 which he had exposed the atrocious
6986𓀴      conduct of Colonel Upwood
 in connection with the famous card
6987𓀴      scandal of the Nonpareil
 Club, while in the second he had
6988𓀴      defended the
 unfortunate Mme.
 Montpensier from the charge of
6989𓀴      murder which hung over her
 in connection with the death of her
6990𓀴      step-daughter,
 Mlle. Carére,
 the young lady who, as it will be
6991𓀴      remembered,
 was found six months
 later alive and married in New
6992𓀴      York. My friend
 was in excellent
 spirits over the success which
6993𓀴      had attended
