 difficult and important cases, so
6994𓀴      that I was able to induce
 him to discuss the details of the
6995𓀴      Baskerville mystery. I had
 waited patiently for the opportunity
6996𓀴      for I was
 aware that he would
 never permit cases to overlap, and
6997𓀴      that his clear and logical
 mind would not be drawn from its
6998𓀴      present work to dwell upon
 memories of the past. Sir Henry and
6999𓀴      Dr. Mortimer were, however,
 in London, on their way to that long
7000𓀴      voyage which
 had been recommended
 for the restoration of his
7001𓀴      shattered nerves.
 They had called
 upon us that very afternoon, so
7002𓀴      that it was natural that
 the subject should come up for
7003𓀴      discussion.
7004𓀴
7005𓀴      “The whole
 course of events,”
 said Holmes, “from the point of
7006𓀴      view of the man who called
 himself Stapleton was simple and
7007𓀴      direct, although to us, who
 had no means in the beginning of
7008𓀴      knowing the motives of his
 actions and could only learn part of
7009𓀴      the facts, it all appeared
 exceedingly complex. I have had the
7010𓀴      advantage of
 two conversations
 with Mrs. Stapleton, and the case
7011𓀴      has now been
 so entirely cleared
 up that I am not aware that
7012𓀴      there is anything which has
 remained a secret to us. You will
7013𓀴      find a few notes upon the
 matter under the heading B in my
7014𓀴      indexed list of cases.”
7015𓀴
7016𓀴      “Perhaps you would kindly
 give me a sketch of the course of
7017𓀴      events from memory.”
7018𓀴
7019𓀴      “Certainly, though I cannot
 guarantee that I carry all the facts
7020𓀴      in my mind. Intense mental
 concentration has a curious way of
7021𓀴      blotting out
 what has passed.
 The barrister who has his case at
7022𓀴      his fingers’
 ends and is able
 to argue with an expert upon his
7023𓀴      own subject
 finds that a week
 or two of the courts will drive it
7024𓀴      all out of
 his head once more.
 So each of my cases displaces the
7025𓀴      last, and Mlle.
 Carére has blurred
 my recollection of Baskerville
7026𓀴      Hall. Tomorrow
 some other little
 problem may be submitted to my
7027𓀴      notice which will
 in turn dispossess
 the fair French lady and the
7028𓀴      infamous Upwood.
 So far as the
 case of the hound goes, however, I
7029𓀴      will give you the course of
 events as nearly as I can, and you
7030𓀴      will suggest anything
 which I may have forgotten.
7031𓀴
7032𓀴      “My inquiries
 show beyond all
 question that the family portrait
7033𓀴      did not lie, and that this
 fellow was indeed a Baskerville. He
7034𓀴      was a son of that
 Rodger Baskerville,
 the younger brother of Sir
7035𓀴      Charles, who
 fled with a sinister
 reputation to South America,
7036𓀴      where he was
 said to have died
 unmarried. He did, as a matter of
7037𓀴      fact, marry,
 and had one child,
 this fellow, whose real name is
7038𓀴      the same as his father’s.
 He married Beryl Garcia, one of the
7039𓀴      beauties of
 Costa Rica, and,
 having purloined a considerable sum
7040𓀴      of public money, he changed
 his name to Vandeleur and fled to
7041𓀴      England, where
 he established
 a school in the east of Yorkshire.
7042𓀴      His reason
 for attempting this
 special line of business was that
7043𓀴      he had struck
 up an acquaintance
 with a consumptive tutor upon
7044𓀴      the voyage
 home, and that he
 had used this man’s ability to make
7045𓀴      the undertaking a success.
 Fraser, the tutor, died however, and
7046𓀴      the school
 which had begun well
 sank from disrepute into infamy.
7047𓀴      The Vandeleurs
 found it convenient
 to change their name to
7048𓀴      Stapleton,
 and he brought the
 remains of his fortune, his schemes
7049𓀴      for the future,
 and his taste
 for entomology to the south of
7050𓀴      England. I learned
 at the British
 Museum that he was a recognized
7051𓀴      authority upon the subject,
 and that the name of Vandeleur has
7052𓀴      been permanently
 attached to
 a certain moth which he had, in his
7053𓀴      Yorkshire days, been
 the first to describe.
7054𓀴
7055𓀴      “We now come
 to that portion
 of his life which has proved to be
7056𓀴      of such intense interest to
 us. The fellow had evidently made
7057𓀴      inquiry and found that only
 two lives intervened between him and
7058𓀴      a valuable estate. When he
 went to Devonshire his plans were, I
7059𓀴      believe, exceedingly hazy,
 but that he meant mischief from the
7060𓀴      first is evident
 from the way
 in which he took his wife with him
7061𓀴      in the character
 of his sister.
 The idea of using her as a decoy
7062𓀴      was clearly already in his
 mind, though he may not have been
7063𓀴      certain how
 the details of his
 plot were to be arranged. He meant
7064𓀴      in the end
 to have the estate,
 and he was ready to use any tool
7065𓀴      or run any
 risk for that end.
 His first act was to establish
7066𓀴      himself as near
 to his ancestral
 home as he could, and his second
7067𓀴      was to cultivate
 a friendship
 with Sir Charles Baskerville and
7068𓀴      with the neighbours.
7069𓀴
7070𓀴      “The baronet himself told
 him about the family hound, and so
7071𓀴      prepared the
 way for his own
 death. Stapleton, as I will continue
7072𓀴      to call him, knew that the
 old man’s heart was weak and that a
7073𓀴      shock would
 kill him. So much
 he had learned from Dr. Mortimer.
7074𓀴      He had heard also that Sir
 Charles was superstitious and had
7075𓀴      taken this grim legend very
 seriously. His ingenious mind
7076𓀴      instantly
 suggested a way by
 which the baronet could be done to
7077𓀴      death, and yet it would be
 hardly possible to bring home the
7078𓀴      guilt to the real murderer.
7079𓀴
7080𓀴      “Having conceived the idea
 he proceeded to carry it out with
7081𓀴      considerable
 finesse. An ordinary
 schemer would have been content
7082𓀴      to work with
 a savage hound.
 The use of artificial means to make
7083𓀴      the creature diabolical was
 a flash of genius upon his part. The
7084𓀴      dog he bought
 in London from
 Ross and Mangles, the dealers in
7085𓀴      Fulham Road.
 It was the strongest
 and most savage in their
7086𓀴      possession.
 He brought it down
 by the North Devon line and walked
7087𓀴      a great distance over the
 moor so as to get it home without
7088𓀴      exciting any
 remarks. He had
 already on his insect hunts learned
7089𓀴      to penetrate the Grimpen
 Mire, and so had found a safe
7090𓀴      hiding-place
 for the creature.
 Here he kennelled it and waited
7091𓀴      his chance.
7092𓀴
7093𓀴      “But it was
 some time coming.
 The old gentleman could not be
7094𓀴      decoyed outside
 of his grounds
 at night. Several times Stapleton
7095𓀴      lurked about
 with his hound,
 but without avail. It was during
7096𓀴      these fruitless quests that
 he, or rather his ally, was seen by
7097𓀴      peasants,
 and that the legend
 of the demon dog received a new
7098𓀴      confirmation. He had hoped
 that his wife might lure Sir Charles
7099𓀴      to his ruin,
 but here she proved
 unexpectedly independent. She
7100𓀴      would not endeavour to
 entangle the old gentleman in a
7101𓀴      sentimental
 attachment which
 might deliver him over to his enemy.
7102𓀴      Threats and
 even, I am sorry
 to say, blows refused to move her.
7103𓀴      She would
 have nothing to do
 with it, and for a time Stapleton
7104𓀴      was at a deadlock.
7105𓀴
7106𓀴      “He found a way out of his
 difficulties through the chance that
7107𓀴      Sir Charles,
 who had conceived
 a friendship for him, made him the
7108𓀴      minister of his charity in
 the case of this unfortunate woman,
7109𓀴      Mrs. Laura Lyons.
 By representing
 himself as a single man he
7110𓀴      acquired complete influence
 over her, and he gave her to
7111𓀴      understand
 that in the event
 of her obtaining a divorce from her
7112𓀴      husband he would marry her.
 His plans were suddenly brought to a
7113𓀴      head by his knowledge that
 Sir Charles was about to leave the
7114𓀴      Hall on the advice
 of Dr. Mortimer,
 with whose opinion he himself
7115𓀴      pretended to
 coincide. He must
 act at once, or his victim might
7116𓀴      get beyond his
 power. He therefore
 put pressure upon Mrs. Lyons
7117𓀴      to write this
 letter, imploring
 the old man to give her an
7118𓀴      interview on
 the evening before
 his departure for London. He
7119𓀴      then, by a
 specious argument,
 prevented her from going, and so
7120𓀴      had the chance
 for which he had waited.
7121𓀴
7122𓀴      “Driving back
 in the evening
 from Coombe Tracey he was in time to
7123𓀴      get his hound,
 to treat it with
 his infernal paint, and to bring
7124𓀴      the beast round to the gate
 at which he had reason to expect that
7125𓀴      he would find
 the old gentleman
 waiting. The dog, incited by its
7126𓀴      master, sprang
 over the wicket-gate
 and pursued the unfortunate
7127𓀴      baronet, who fled screaming
 down the yew alley. In that gloomy
7128𓀴      tunnel it must
 indeed have been
 a dreadful sight to see that huge
7129𓀴      black creature,
 with its flaming
 jaws and blazing eyes, bounding
7130𓀴      after its victim.
 He fell dead
 at the end of the alley from heart
7131𓀴      disease and
 terror. The hound
 had kept upon the grassy border
7132𓀴      while the baronet
 had run down
 the path, so that no track but the
7133𓀴      man’s was
 visible. On seeing
 him lying still the creature had
7134𓀴      probably approached
 to sniff
 at him, but finding him dead had
7135𓀴      turned away
 again. It was then
 that it left the print which was
7136𓀴      actually observed
 by Dr. Mortimer.
 The hound was called off and
7137𓀴      hurried away to its lair in
 the Grimpen Mire, and a mystery was
7138𓀴      left which puzzled
 the authorities,
 alarmed the countryside, and
7139𓀴      finally brought
 the case within
 the scope of our observation.
7140𓀴
7141𓀴      “So much for
 the death of Sir
 Charles Baskerville. You perceive
7142𓀴      the devilish cunning of
 it, for really it would be almost
7143𓀴      impossible to make a case
 against the real murderer. His only
7144𓀴      accomplice
 was one who could
 never give him away, and the
7145𓀴      grotesque,
 inconceivable nature
 of the device only served to make
7146𓀴      it more effective.
 Both of the
 women concerned in the case, Mrs.
7147𓀴      Stapleton and
 Mrs. Laura Lyons,
 were left with a strong suspicion
7148𓀴      against Stapleton.
 Mrs. Stapleton
 knew that he had designs upon
7149𓀴      the old man,
 and also of the
 existence of the hound. Mrs. Lyons
7150𓀴      knew neither
 of these things,
 but had been impressed by the death
7151𓀴      occurring at the time of an
 uncancelled appointment which was
7152𓀴      only known to him. However,
 both of them were under his
7153𓀴      influence,
 and he had nothing
 to fear from them. The first half
7154𓀴      of his task
 was successfully
 accomplished but the more difficult
7155𓀴      still remained.
7156𓀴
7157𓀴      “It is possible
 that Stapleton
 did not know of the existence of
7158𓀴      an heir in
 Canada. In any case
 he would very soon learn it from
7159𓀴      his friend Dr. Mortimer,
 and he was told by the latter all
7160𓀴      details about
 the arrival of
 Henry Baskerville. Stapleton’s first
7161𓀴      idea was that
 this young stranger
 from Canada might possibly be
7162𓀴      done to death
 in London without
 coming down to Devonshire at all.
7163𓀴      He distrusted his wife ever
 since she had refused to help him in
7164𓀴      laying a trap
 for the old man,
 and he dared not leave her long
7165𓀴      out of his
 sight for fear he
 should lose his influence over her.
7166𓀴      It was for this reason that
 he took her to London with him. They
7167𓀴      lodged, I find,
 at the Mexborough
 Private Hotel, in Craven
7168𓀴      Street, which was actually
 one of those called upon by my agent
7169𓀴      in search of evidence. Here
 he kept his wife imprisoned in her
7170𓀴      room while he, disguised in
 a beard, followed Dr. Mortimer to
7171𓀴      Baker Street and afterwards
 to the station and to the
7172𓀴      Northumberland
 Hotel. His wife
 had some inkling of his plans; but
7173𓀴      she had such a fear of her
 husband—a fear founded upon brutal
7174𓀴      ill-treatment—that she dare
 not write to warn the man whom she
7175𓀴      knew to be
 in danger. If the
 letter should fall into Stapleton’s
7176𓀴      hands her
 own life would not
 be safe. Eventually, as we know, she
7177𓀴      adopted the
 expedient of cutting
 out the words which would form
7178𓀴      the message, and addressing
 the letter in a disguised hand. It
7179𓀴      reached the baronet, and
 gave him the first warning of his
7180𓀴      danger.
7181𓀴
7182𓀴      “It was very essential for
 Stapleton to get some article of Sir
7183𓀴      Henry’s attire
 so that, in case
 he was driven to use the dog, he
7184𓀴      might always have the means
 of setting him upon his track. With
7185𓀴      characteristic
 promptness and
 audacity he set about this at once,
7186𓀴      and we cannot
 doubt that the
 boots or chamber-maid of the hotel
7187𓀴      was well bribed to help him
 in his design. By chance, however,
7188𓀴      the first boot
 which was procured
 for him was a new one and,
7189𓀴      therefore, useless for his
 purpose. He then had it returned and
7190𓀴      obtained another—a
 most instructive
 incident, since it proved
7191𓀴      conclusively
 to my mind that
 we were dealing with a real hound,
7192𓀴      as no other
 supposition could
 explain this anxiety to obtain an
7193𓀴      old boot and
 this indifference
 to a new one. The more _outré_ and
7194𓀴      grotesque
 an incident is the
 more carefully it deserves to be
7195𓀴      examined,
 and the very point
 which appears to complicate a case
7196𓀴      is, when duly
 considered and
 scientifically handled, the one
7197𓀴      which is most
 likely to elucidate it.
7198𓀴
7199𓀴      “Then we had the visit from
 our friends next morning, shadowed
7200𓀴      always by Stapleton in the
 cab. From his knowledge of our rooms
7201𓀴      and of my
 appearance, as well
 as from his general conduct, I am
7202𓀴      inclined to think
 that Stapleton’s
 career of crime has been by no
7203𓀴      means limited
 to this single
 Baskerville affair. It is suggestive
7204𓀴      that during the last three
 years there have been four
7205𓀴      considerable burglaries in
 the west country, for none of which
7206𓀴      was any criminal
 ever arrested.
 The last of these, at Folkestone
7207𓀴      Court, in
 May, was remarkable
 for the cold-blooded pistolling of
7208𓀴      the page, who surprised the
 masked and solitary burglar. I cannot
7209𓀴      doubt that
 Stapleton recruited
 his waning resources in this
7210𓀴      fashion, and that for years
 he has been a desperate and dangerous
7211𓀴      man.
7212𓀴
7213𓀴      “We had an example
 of his readiness
 of resource that morning when
7214𓀴      he got away from
 us so successfully,
 and also of his audacity in
7215𓀴      sending back my own name to
 me through the cabman. From that
7216𓀴      moment he understood that I
 had taken over the case in London,
7217𓀴      and that therefore
 there was
 no chance for him there. He returned
7218𓀴      to Dartmoor and awaited
 the arrival of the baronet.”
7219𓀴
7220𓀴      “One moment!”
 said I. “You have,
 no doubt, described the sequence
7221𓀴      of events correctly,
 but there
 is one point which you have left
7222𓀴      unexplained. What became of
 the hound when its master was in
7223𓀴      London?”
7224𓀴
7225𓀴      “I have given
 some attention
 to this matter and it is undoubtedly
7226𓀴      of importance. There can be
 no question that Stapleton had a
7227𓀴      confidant, though
 it is unlikely
 that he ever placed himself in
7228𓀴      his power
 by sharing all his
 plans with him. There was an old
7229𓀴      manservant
 at Merripit House,
 whose name was Anthony. His
7230𓀴      connection
 with the Stapletons
 can be traced for several years,
7231𓀴      as far back as
 the school-mastering
 days, so that he must have
7232𓀴      been aware that his master
 and mistress were really husband and
7233𓀴      wife. This
 man has disappeared
 and has escaped from the country.
7234𓀴      It is suggestive
 that Anthony
 is not a common name in England,
7235𓀴      while Antonio
 is so in all Spanish
 or Spanish-American countries.
7236𓀴      The man, like
 Mrs. Stapleton
 herself, spoke good English, but
7237𓀴      with a curious
 lisping accent.
 I have myself seen this old man
7238𓀴      cross the Grimpen Mire by
 the path which Stapleton had marked
7239𓀴      out. It is very
 probable, therefore,
 that in the absence of his
7240𓀴      master it was he who cared
 for the hound, though he may never
7241𓀴      have known the purpose
 for which the beast was used.
7242𓀴
7243𓀴      “The Stapletons
 then went down
 to Devonshire, whither they were
7244𓀴      soon followed by Sir Henry
 and you. One word now as to how I
7245𓀴      stood myself at that time.
 It may possibly recur to your memory
7246𓀴      that when I
 examined the paper
 upon which the printed words were
7247𓀴      fastened I made
 a close inspection
 for the water-mark. In doing
7248𓀴      so I held it
 within a few inches
 of my eyes, and was conscious of
7249𓀴      a faint smell of the scent
 known as white jessamine. There are
7250𓀴      seventy-five
 perfumes, which
 it is very necessary that a criminal
7251𓀴      expert should
 be able to distinguish
 from each other, and cases
7252𓀴      have more
 than once within my
 own experience depended upon their
7253𓀴      prompt recognition.
 The scent
 suggested the presence of a lady,
7254𓀴      and already
 my thoughts began
 to turn towards the Stapletons.
7255𓀴      Thus I had made certain of
 the hound, and had guessed at the
7256𓀴      criminal before ever
 we went to the west country.
7257𓀴
7258𓀴      “It was my game
 to watch Stapleton.
 It was evident, however, that
7259𓀴      I could not
 do this if I were
 with you, since he would be keenly
7260𓀴      on his guard.
 I deceived everybody,
 therefore, yourself included,
7261𓀴      and I came
 down secretly when
 I was supposed to be in London. My
7262𓀴      hardships were not so great
 as you imagined, though such trifling
7263𓀴      details must
 never interfere
 with the investigation of a case. I
7264𓀴      stayed for the most part at
 Coombe Tracey, and only used the hut
7265𓀴      upon the moor when it was
 necessary to be near the scene of
7266𓀴      action. Cartwright had come
 down with me, and in his disguise as
7267𓀴      a country
 boy he was of great
 assistance to me. I was dependent
7268𓀴      upon him for food and clean
 linen. When I was watching Stapleton,
7269𓀴      Cartwright was frequently
 watching you, so that I was able to
7270𓀴      keep my hand
 upon all the strings.
7271𓀴
7272𓀴      “I have already
 told you that
 your reports reached me rapidly,
7273𓀴      being forwarded instantly
 from Baker Street to Coombe Tracey.
7274𓀴      They were of great service
 to me, and especially that one
7275𓀴      incidentally truthful piece
 of biography of Stapleton’s. I was
7276𓀴      able to establish
 the identity
 of the man and the woman and knew
7277𓀴      at last exactly
 how I stood.
 The case had been considerably
7278𓀴      complicated
 through the incident
 of the escaped convict and the
7279𓀴      relations
 between him and the
 Barrymores. This also you cleared
7280𓀴      up in a very effective way,
 though I had already come to the same
7281𓀴      conclusions from
 my own observations.
7282𓀴
7283𓀴      “By the time
 that you discovered
 me upon the moor I had a
7284𓀴      complete knowledge
 of the whole
 business, but I had not a case
7285𓀴      which could
 go to a jury. Even
 Stapleton’s attempt upon Sir Henry
7286𓀴      that night
 which ended in the
 death of the unfortunate convict
7287𓀴      did not help
 us much in proving
 murder against our man. There
7288𓀴      seemed to be no alternative
 but to catch him red-handed, and to
7289𓀴      do so we had
 to use Sir Henry,
 alone and apparently unprotected,
7290𓀴      as a bait.
 We did so, and at
 the cost of a severe shock to our
7291𓀴      client we succeeded
 in completing
 our case and driving Stapleton
7292𓀴      to his destruction.
 That Sir
 Henry should have been exposed to
7293𓀴      this is, I must
 confess, a reproach
 to my management of the case,
7294𓀴      but we had no
 means of foreseeing
 the terrible and paralyzing
7295𓀴      spectacle which
 the beast presented,
 nor could we predict the fog
7296𓀴      which enabled him to burst
 upon us at such short notice. We
7297𓀴      succeeded in our object at
 a cost which both the specialist and
7298𓀴      Dr. Mortimer assure me will
 be a temporary one. A long journey
7299𓀴      may enable our
 friend to recover
 not only from his shattered
7300𓀴      nerves but also
 from his wounded
 feelings. His love for the lady
7301𓀴      was deep and sincere, and
 to him the saddest part of all this
7302𓀴      black business was that he
 should have been deceived by her.
7303𓀴
7304𓀴      “It only remains
 to indicate
 the part which she had played
7305𓀴      throughout. There can be no
 doubt that Stapleton exercised an
7306𓀴      influence
 over her which may
 have been love or may have been
7307𓀴      fear, or very possibly
 both, since they are by no means
7308𓀴      incompatible
 emotions. It was,
 at least, absolutely effective. At
7309𓀴      his command
 she consented to
 pass as his sister, though he found
7310𓀴      the limits
 of his power over
 her when he endeavoured to make her
7311𓀴      the direct
 accessory to murder.
 She was ready to warn Sir Henry
7312𓀴      so far as she could without
 implicating her husband, and again
7313𓀴      and again she tried to do
 so. Stapleton himself seems to have
7314𓀴      been capable of jealousy,
 and when he saw the baronet paying
7315𓀴      court to the
 lady, even though
 it was part of his own plan, still
7316𓀴      he could not
 help interrupting
 with a passionate outburst which
7317𓀴      revealed the
 fiery soul which
 his self-contained manner so
7318𓀴      cleverly concealed.
 By encouraging
 the intimacy he made it
7319𓀴      certain that
 Sir Henry would
 frequently come to Merripit House
7320𓀴      and that he would sooner or
 later get the opportunity which he
7321𓀴      desired. On the day of the
 crisis, however, his wife turned
7322𓀴      suddenly against
 him. She had
 learned something of the death of
7323𓀴      the convict,
 and she knew that
 the hound was being kept in the
7324𓀴      outhouse on
 the evening that
 Sir Henry was coming to dinner. She
7325𓀴      taxed her husband with his
 intended crime, and a furious scene
7326𓀴      followed in which he showed
 her for the first time that she had a
7327𓀴      rival in his
 love. Her fidelity
 turned in an instant to bitter
7328𓀴      hatred, and he saw that she
 would betray him. He tied her up,
7329𓀴      therefore,
 that she might have
 no chance of warning Sir Henry,
7330𓀴      and he hoped,
 no doubt, that
 when the whole countryside put down
7331𓀴      the baronet’s
 death to the curse
 of his family, as they certainly
7332𓀴      would do, he could win his
 wife back to accept an accomplished
7333𓀴      fact and to
 keep silent upon
 what she knew. In this I fancy that
7334𓀴      in any case he
 made a miscalculation,
 and that, if we had not
7335𓀴      been there, his doom would
 none the less have been sealed. A
7336𓀴      woman of Spanish blood does
 not condone such an injury so
7337𓀴      lightly. And
 now, my dear Watson,
 without referring to my notes,
7338𓀴      I cannot give
 you a more detailed
 account of this curious case. I
7339𓀴      do not know that
 anything essential
 has been left unexplained.”
7340𓀴
7341𓀴      “He could not
 hope to frighten
 Sir Henry to death as he had done
7342𓀴      the old uncle
 with his bogie hound.”
7343𓀴
7344𓀴      “The beast was
 savage and half-starved.
 If its appearance did not
7345𓀴      frighten its
 victim to death,
 at least it would paralyze the
7346𓀴      resistance which
 might be offered.”
7347𓀴
7348𓀴      “No doubt.
 There only remains
 one difficulty. If Stapleton came
7349𓀴      into the succession,
 how could
 he explain the fact that he, the
7350𓀴      heir, had been
 living unannounced
 under another name so close to
7351𓀴      the property?
 How could he claim
 it without causing suspicion and
7352𓀴      inquiry?”
7353𓀴
7354𓀴      “It is a formidable
 difficulty,
 and I fear that you ask too much
7355𓀴      when you expect me to solve
 it. The past and the present are
7356𓀴      within the
 field of my inquiry,
 but what a man may do in the
7357𓀴      future is
 a hard question to
 answer. Mrs. Stapleton has heard her
7358𓀴      husband discuss the problem
 on several occasions. There were
7359𓀴      three possible courses. He
 might claim the property from South
7360𓀴      America, establish
 his identity
 before the British authorities
7361𓀴      there and so
 obtain the fortune
 without ever coming to England at
7362𓀴      all, or he might adopt an
 elaborate disguise during the short
7363𓀴      time that he
 need be in London;
 or, again, he might furnish an
7364𓀴      accomplice with the proofs
 and papers, putting him in as heir,
7365𓀴      and retaining a claim upon
 some proportion of his income. We
7366𓀴      cannot doubt
 from what we know
 of him that he would have found
7367𓀴      some way out
 of the difficulty.
 And now, my dear Watson, we have
7368𓀴      had some weeks
 of severe work,
 and for one evening, I think, we
7369𓀴      may turn our thoughts into
 more pleasant channels. I have a box
7370𓀴      for _Les Huguenots_. Have
 you heard the De Reszkes? Might I
7371𓀴      trouble you
 then to be ready
 in half an hour, and we can stop at
7372𓀴      Marcini’s for a little
 dinner on the way?”
7373𓀴
7374𓀴
7375𓀴THE END
7376𓀴
7377𓀴
